          <span class="metadata">
           <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Star_of_David.svg">
            <img alt="Portal icon" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="260" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Star_of_David.svg/14px-Star_of_David.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Star_of_David.svg/21px-Star_of_David.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Star_of_David.svg/28px-Star_of_David.svg.png 2x" width="14"/>

     <p>
      <b>
       Kabbalah
      </b>
      (Hebrew:
      <span dir="rtl" lang="he" xml:lang="he">
       
      </span>
      , literally "receiving/tradition"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      ) is an
      <a href="/wiki/Esotericism" title="Esotericism">
       esoteric
      </a>
      method, discipline, and school of thought that originated in Judaism. A traditional Kabbalist in Judaism is called a
      <i>
       Mekubbal
      </i>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">
       Hebrew
      </a>
      :
      <span dir="rtl" lang="he" xml:lang="he">
       
      </span>
      ).
     </p>
     <p>
      Kabbalah's definition varies according to the tradition and aims of those following it,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      from its religious origin as an integral part of Judaism, to its later
      <a href="/wiki/Christian_Kabbalah" title="Christian Kabbalah">
       Christian
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/New_Age" title="New Age">
       New Age
      </a>
      , and Occultist syncretic adaptations. Kabbalah is a set of esoteric teachings meant to explain the relationship between an unchanging, eternal, and mysterious
      <a href="/wiki/Ein_Sof" title="Ein Sof">
       Ein Sof
      </a>
      (infinity)
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and the mortal and finite universe (God's creation). While it is heavily used by some denominations, it is not a religious denomination in itself. It forms the foundations of mystical religious interpretation. Kabbalah seeks to define the nature of the universe and the human being, the nature and purpose of existence, and various other
      <a href="/wiki/Ontology" title="Ontology">
       ontological
      </a>
      questions. It also presents methods to aid understanding of the concepts and thereby attain spiritual realisation.
     </p>
     <p>
      Kabbalah originally developed entirely within the realm of
      <a href="/wiki/Jewish_thought" title="Jewish thought">
       Jewish thought
      </a>
      , and kabbalists often use classical Jewish sources to explain and demonstrate its esoteric teachings. These teachings are held by followers in Judaism to define the inner meaning of both the
      <a href="/wiki/Tanakh" title="Tanakh">
       Hebrew Bible
      </a>
      and traditional
      <a href="/wiki/Rabbinic_literature" title="Rabbinic literature">
       Rabbinic literature
      </a>
      and their formerly concealed transmitted dimension, as well as to explain the significance of Jewish religious observances.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Traditional practitioners believe its earliest origins pre-date world religions, forming the primordial blueprint for Creation's philosophies, religions, sciences, arts, and political systems.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Historically, Kabbalah emerged, after earlier forms of
      <a href="/wiki/Jewish_mysticism" title="Jewish mysticism">
       Jewish mysticism
      </a>
      , in 12th- to 13th-century Southern France and Spain, becoming reinterpreted in the Jewish mystical renaissance of 16th-century
      <a href="/wiki/Safed" title="Safed">
       Ottoman Palestine
      </a>
      . It was popularised in the form of Hasidic Judaism from the 18th century onwards. Twentieth-century interest in Kabbalah has inspired cross-denominational
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jewish_renewal" title="Jewish renewal">
       Jewish renewal
      </a>
      and contributed to wider non-Jewish contemporary spirituality, as well as engaging its flourishing emergence and historical re-emphasis through newly established academic investigation.
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

     <p>

     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">
       Overview
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kabbalah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Overview">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      According to the
      <a href="/wiki/Zohar" title="Zohar">
       Zohar
      </a>
      , a foundational text for kabbalistic thought,
      <a href="/wiki/Torah_study" title="Torah study">
       Torah study
      </a>
      can proceed along four levels of interpretation (
      <a href="/wiki/Exegesis" title="Exegesis">
       exegesis
      </a>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      These four levels are called
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Pardes_(Jewish_exegesis)" title="Pardes (Jewish exegesis)">
        pardes
       </a>
      </i>
      from their initial letters (PRDS Hebrew:
      <span dir="rtl" lang="he" xml:lang="he">
       
      </span>
      , orchard).
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Peshat" title="Peshat">
         Peshat
        </a>
       </i>
       (Hebrew:
       <span dir="rtl" lang="he" xml:lang="he">
        
       </span>
        lit. "simple"): the direct interpretations of meaning.
      </li>
      <li>
       <i>
        Remez
       </i>
       (Hebrew:
       <span dir="rtl" lang="he" xml:lang="he">
        
       </span>
        lit. "hint[s]"): the
       <a href="/wiki/Allegory" title="Allegory">
        allegoric
       </a>
       meanings (through
       <a href="/wiki/Allusion" title="Allusion">
        allusion
       </a>
       ).
      </li>
      <li>
       <i>
        Derash
       </i>
       (Hebrew:
       <span dir="rtl" lang="he" xml:lang="he">
        
       </span>
        from Heb.
       <i>
        darash
       </i>
       : "inquire" or "seek"):
       <a href="/wiki/Midrash" title="Midrash">
        midrashic
       </a>
       (Rabbinic) meanings, often with imaginative comparisons with similar words or verses.
      </li>
      <li>
       <i>
        Sod
       </i>
       (Hebrew:
       <span dir="rtl" lang="he" xml:lang="he">
        
       </span>
        lit. "secret" or "mystery"): the inner, esoteric (
       <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">
        metaphysical
       </a>
       ) meanings, expressed in kabbalah.
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      Kabbalah is considered by its followers as a necessary part of the study of
      <a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">
       Torah
      </a>
       the study of Torah (the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Tanakh" title="Tanakh">
        Tanakh
       </a>
      </i>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Rabbinic_literature" title="Rabbinic literature">
       Rabbinic literature
      </a>
      ) being an inherent duty of observant Jews.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Kabbalah teaches doctrines that are accepted by some Jews as the true meaning of Judaism while other Jews have rejected these doctrines as heretical and antithetical to Judaism. After the Medieval Kabbalah, and especially after its 16th-century development and synthesis, Kabbalah replaced
      <a href="/wiki/Jewish_philosophy" title="Jewish philosophy">
       Jewish philosophy
      </a>
      (
      <i>
       hakira
      </i>
      ) as the mainstream traditional Jewish theology,
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2012)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      both in scholarly circles and in the popular imagination. With the arrival of modernity,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nola.com_9-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-nola.com-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      through the influence of
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Haskalah" title="Haskalah">
        haskalah
       </a>
      </i>
      , this has changed among non-Orthodox Jewish denominations, although its 20th-century academic study and cross-denominational spiritual applications (especially through
      <a href="/wiki/Neo-Hasidism" title="Neo-Hasidism">
       Neo-Hasidism
      </a>
      ) has reawakened a following beyond Orthodoxy.
     </p>
     <p>
      Scholars dispute whether the term "kabbalah" originated with the Jewish philosopher
      <a href="/wiki/Solomon_ibn_Gabirol" title="Solomon ibn Gabirol">
       Solomon ibn Gabirol
      </a>
      (10211058) or with the 13th-century Spanish kabbalist
      <a href="/wiki/Bahya_ben_Asher" title="Bahya ben Asher">
       Bahya ben Asher
      </a>
      . While other terms have been used in many religious documents from the 2nd century up to the present day, the term "kabbalah" has become the main descriptive of Jewish esoteric knowledge and practices.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2012)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      Jewish mystical literature, which served as the basis for the development of kabbalistic thought, developed through a theological tradition inherent in Judaism from Antiquity, as part of wider Rabbinic literature. Its theoretical development can be characterised in alternative schools and successive stages. After the
      <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Bible" title="Hebrew Bible">
       Hebrew Bible
      </a>
      experience of prophecy, the first documented schools of specifically mystical theory and method in Judaism are found in the 1st and 2nd centuries, described in the
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heichalot" title="Heichalot">
        heichalot
       </a>
      </i>
      (supernal "palaces") texts and the earliest existent book on Jewish esotericism,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Sefer_Yetzirah" title="Sefer Yetzirah">
        Sefer Yetzirah
       </a>
      </i>
      . Their method, known as
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Merkabah" title="Merkabah">
        Merkabah
       </a>
      </i>
      (contemplation of the Divine "Chariot") mysticism lasted until the 10th century, where it was subsumed by the Medieval doctrinal emergence of the Kabbalah in southwestern Europe in the 12th and 13th centuries. Its teachings, embodied in the
      <i>
       Zohar
      </i>
      , became the foundation of later Jewish mysticism, becoming re-interpreted in the early-modern developments of 16th-century
      <a href="/wiki/Safed" title="Safed">
       Safed
      </a>
      in the Galilee, through the new system of
      <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Luria" title="Isaac Luria">
       Isaac Luria
      </a>
      .
      <a href="/wiki/Lurianic_Kabbalah" title="Lurianic Kabbalah">
       Lurianic Kabbalah
      </a>
      became popularised as a social mysticism for the whole Jewish community through 18th-century Hasidism in eastern Europe, and its new notions of mystical leadership.
     </p>
     <p>
      Modern academic-historical study of Jewish mysticism reserves the term "kabbalah" to designate the particular, distinctive doctrines that textually emerged fully expressed in the Middle Ages, as distinct from the earlier Merkabah mystical concepts and methods.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      According to this descriptive categorisation, both versions of Kabbalistic theory, the medieval-Zoharic and the early-modern Lurianic together comprise the theosophical tradition in Kabbalah, while the meditative-ecstatic Kabbalah incorporates a parallel inter-related Medieval tradition. A third tradition, related but more shunned, involves the magical aims of
      <a href="/wiki/Practical_Kabbalah" title="Practical Kabbalah">
       Practical Kabbalah
      </a>
      . Moshe Idel, for example, writes that these 3 basic models can be discerned operating and competing throughout the whole history of Jewish mysticism, beyond the particular Kabbalistic background of the Middle Ages.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      They can be readily distinguished by their basic intent with respect to God:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       The Theosophical tradition of
       <i>
        Theoretical Kabbalah
       </i>
       (the main focus of the Zohar and Luria) seeks to understand and describe the divine realm. As an alternative to rationalist Jewish philosophy, particularly Maimonides' Aristotelianism, this speculation became the central component of Kabbalah
      </li>
      <li>
       The Ecstatic tradition of
       <i>
        Meditative Kabbalah
       </i>
       (exemplified by
       <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Abulafia" title="Abraham Abulafia">
        Abulafia
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isaac_of_Acre" title="Isaac of Acre">
        Isaac of Acre
       </a>
       ) strives to achieve a mystical union with God. Abraham Abulafia's "Prophetic Kabbalah" was the supreme example of this, though marginal in Kabbalistic development, and his alternative to the program of theosophical Kabbalah
      </li>
      <li>
       The Magico-theurgical tradition of
       <i>
        Practical Kabbalah
       </i>
       (in often unpublished manuscripts) endeavours to alter both the Divine realms and the World. While some interpretations of prayer see its role as manipulating heavenly forces, Practical Kabbalah properly involved white-magical acts, and was censored by kabbalists for only those completely pure of intent. Consequently it formed a separate minor tradition shunned from Kabbalah
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      According to traditional belief, early kabbalistic knowledge was transmitted orally by the Patriarchs,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neviim" title="Neviim">
       prophets
      </a>
      , and sages (hakhamim in Hebrew), eventually to be "interwoven" into Jewish religious writings and culture. According to this view, early kabbalah was, in around the 10th century BC, an open knowledge practiced by over a million people in ancient Israel.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Foreign conquests drove the Jewish spiritual leadership of the time (the
      <a href="/wiki/Sanhedrin" title="Sanhedrin">
       Sanhedrin
      </a>
      ) to hide the knowledge and make it secret, fearing that it might be misused if it fell into the wrong hands.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
       <a href="#cite_note-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Sanhedrin leaders were also concerned that the practice of kabbalah by Jews of the
      <a href="/wiki/Jewish_diaspora" title="Jewish diaspora">
       Jewish diaspora
      </a>
      , unsupervised and unguided by the masters, might lead them into wrong practice and forbidden ways. As a result, the kabbalah became secretive, forbidden and esoteric to Judaism (
      <i>
       Torat Ha'Sod
      </i>
      <span lang="he" xml:lang="he">
        
      </span>
      ) for two and a half millennia.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2012)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      It is hard to clarify with any degree of certainty the exact concepts within kabbalah. There are several different schools of thought with very different outlooks; however, all are accepted as correct.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Modern
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Halakhic" title="Halakhic">
       halakhic
      </a>
      authorities have tried to narrow the scope and diversity within kabbalah, by restricting study to certain texts, notably Zohar and the teachings of Isaac Luria as passed down through
      <a href="/wiki/Hayyim_ben_Joseph_Vital" title="Hayyim ben Joseph Vital">
       Hayyim ben Joseph Vital
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
       <a href="#cite_note-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However even this qualification does little to limit the scope of understanding and expression, as included in those works are commentaries on Abulafian writings,
      <i>
       Sefer Yetzirah
      </i>
      , Albotonian writings, and the
      <i>
       Berit Menuhah
      </i>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
       <a href="#cite_note-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      which is known to the kabbalistic elect and which, as described more recently by
      <a href="/wiki/Gershom_Scholem" title="Gershom Scholem">
       Gershom Scholem
      </a>
      , combined ecstatic with theosophical mysticism. It is therefore important to bear in mind when discussing things such as the
      <i>
       sefirot
      </i>
      and their interactions that one is dealing with highly abstract concepts that at best can only be understood intuitively.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
       <a href="#cite_note-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Difference_between_Jewish_and_non-Jewish_Kabbalah">
       Difference between Jewish and non-Jewish Kabbalah
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kabbalah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Difference between Jewish and non-Jewish Kabbalah">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Portae_Lucis,_Joseph_Gikatilla.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="1558" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Portae_Lucis%2C_Joseph_Gikatilla.jpg/120px-Portae_Lucis%2C_Joseph_Gikatilla.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Portae_Lucis%2C_Joseph_Gikatilla.jpg/180px-Portae_Lucis%2C_Joseph_Gikatilla.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Portae_Lucis%2C_Joseph_Gikatilla.jpg/240px-Portae_Lucis%2C_Joseph_Gikatilla.jpg 2x" width="120"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Portae_Lucis,_Joseph_Gikatilla.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Latin translation of
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joseph_Gikatilla" title="Joseph Gikatilla">
         Gikatilla's
        </a>
        <i>
         Shaarei Ora
        </i>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      From the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">
       Renaissance
      </a>
      onwards Jewish Kabbalah texts entered non-Jewish culture, where they were studied and translated by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_Hebraists" title="Christian Hebraists">
       Christian Hebraists
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Hermeticism" title="Hermeticism">
       Hermetic
      </a>
      occultists.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
       <a href="#cite_note-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Syncretism" title="Syncretism">
       Syncretic
      </a>
      traditions of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_Cabala" title="Christian Cabala">
       Christian Cabala
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Hermetic_Qabalah" title="Hermetic Qabalah">
       Hermetic Qabalah
      </a>
      developed independently of Jewish Kabbalah, reading the Jewish texts as universal ancient wisdom. Both adapted the Jewish concepts freely from their Judaic understanding, to merge with other theologies, religious traditions and magical associations. With the decline of Christian Cabala in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Age_of_Reason" title="Age of Reason">
       Age of Reason
      </a>
      , Hermetic Qabalah continued as a central underground tradition in
      <a href="/wiki/Western_esotericism" title="Western esotericism">
       Western esotericism
      </a>
      . Through these non-Jewish associations with magic,
      <a href="/wiki/Alchemy" title="Alchemy">
       alchemy
      </a>
      and divination, Kabbalah acquired some popular
      <a href="/wiki/Occult" title="Occult">
       occult
      </a>
      connotations forbidden within Judaism, where Jewish theurgic Practical Kabbalah was a minor, permitted tradition restricted for a few elite. Today, many publications on Kabbalah belong to the non-Jewish New Age and occult traditions of Cabala, rather than giving an accurate picture of Judaic Kabbalah.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
       <a href="#cite_note-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Instead, academic and traditional publications now translate and study Judaic Kabbalah for wide readership.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_Jewish_mysticism">
       History of Jewish mysticism
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kabbalah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: History of Jewish mysticism">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em;background:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;padding:0.2em;border-spacing:0.4em 0;text-align:center;line-height:1.4em;font-size:88%">
      <tr>

        </a>
       </th>
      </tr>

           </tr>
           <tr style="vertical-align:top">
            <td style="text-align:left;">
             <a href="/wiki/Neo-Hasidism" title="Neo-Hasidism">

             </a>
            </td>

             c. 1900stoday
            </td>
           </tr>
           <tr style="vertical-align:top">

             </a>
            </td>

             c. 1910stoday
            </td>

           <tr style="vertical-align:top">

             </a>
            </td>

         </div>
        </div>
       </td>
      </tr>

     </table>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Origins_of_Judaic_mysticism">
       Origins of Judaic mysticism
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kabbalah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Origins of Judaic mysticism">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      According to the traditional understanding, Kabbalah dates from Eden.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
       <a href="#cite_note-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It came down from a remote past as a revelation to elect
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tzadikim" title="Tzadikim">
       Tzadikim
      </a>
      (righteous people), and, for the most part, was preserved only by a privileged few. Talmudic Judaism records its view of the proper protocol for teaching this wisdom, as well as many of its concepts, in the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Talmud" title="Talmud">
        Talmud
       </a>
      </i>
      , Tractate
      <i>
       Hagigah
      </i>
      , Ch.2.
     </p>
     <p>
      Contemporary scholarship suggests that various schools of Jewish esotericism arose at different periods of Jewish history, each reflecting not only prior forms of
      <a href="/wiki/Mysticism" title="Mysticism">
       mysticism
      </a>
      , but also the intellectual and cultural milieu of that historical period. Answers to questions of transmission, lineage, influence, and innovation vary greatly and cannot be easily summarised.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Origins_of_terms">
       Origins of terms
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kabbalah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Origins of terms">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Originally, Kabbalistic knowledge was believed to be an integral part of the
      <a href="/wiki/Oral_Torah" title="Oral Torah">
       Oral Torah
      </a>
      , given by God to
      <a href="/wiki/Moses" title="Moses">
       Moses
      </a>
      on
      <a href="/wiki/Mount_Sinai" title="Mount Sinai">
       Mount Sinai
      </a>
      around the 13th century BCE, though there is a view that Kabbalah began with Adam.
     </p>
     <p>
      When the Israelites arrived at their destination and settled in
      <a href="/wiki/Canaan" title="Canaan">
       Canaan
      </a>
      , for a few centuries the esoteric knowledge was referred to by its aspect practicemeditation
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hitbonenut" title="Hitbonenut">
       Hitbonenut
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">
       Hebrew
      </a>
      :
      <span dir="rtl" lang="he" xml:lang="he">
       
      </span>
      ),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
       <a href="#cite_note-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Rebbe
      <a href="/wiki/Nachman_of_Breslov" title="Nachman of Breslov">
       Nachman of Breslov
      </a>
      's
      <a href="/wiki/Hitbodedut" title="Hitbodedut">
       Hitbodedut
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">
       Hebrew
      </a>
      :
      <span dir="rtl" lang="he" xml:lang="he">
       
      </span>
      ), translated as "being alone" or "isolating oneself", or by a different term describing the actual, desired goal of the practiceprophecy ("
      <i>
       NeVu'a
      </i>
      "
      <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">
       Hebrew
      </a>
      :
      <span dir="rtl" lang="he" xml:lang="he">
       
      </span>
      ).
     </p>
     <p>
      During the 5th century BCE, when the works of the Tanakh were edited and canonised and the secret knowledge encrypted within the various writings and scrolls ("Megilot"), the knowledge was referred to as
      <i>
       Ma'aseh Merkavah
      </i>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">
       Hebrew
      </a>
      :
      <span dir="rtl" lang="he" xml:lang="he">
        
      </span>
      )
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
       <a href="#cite_note-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and
      <i>
       Ma'aseh B'reshit
      </i>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">
       Hebrew
      </a>
      :
      <span dir="rtl" lang="he" xml:lang="he">
        
      </span>
      ),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
       <a href="#cite_note-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      respectively "the act of the Chariot" and "the act of Creation". Merkavah mysticism alluded to the encrypted knowledge within the book of the prophet
      <a href="/wiki/Ezekiel" title="Ezekiel">
       Ezekiel
      </a>
      describing his vision of the "Divine Chariot". B'reshit mysticism referred to the first chapter of
      <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Genesis" title="Book of Genesis">
       Genesis
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">
       Hebrew
      </a>
      :
      <span dir="rtl" lang="he" xml:lang="he">
       
      </span>
      ) in the Torah that is believed to contain secrets of the creation of the universe and forces of nature. These terms are also mentioned in the second chapter of the Talmudic tractate
      <i>
       Haggigah
      </i>
      .
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Mystic_elements_of_the_Torah">
       Mystic elements of the Torah
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kabbalah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Mystic elements of the Torah">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:142px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ark_of_the_Covenant_19th-century.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="259" data-file-width="409" height="89" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/2b/Ark_of_the_Covenant_19th-century.png/140px-Ark_of_the_Covenant_19th-century.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/2b/Ark_of_the_Covenant_19th-century.png/210px-Ark_of_the_Covenant_19th-century.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/2b/Ark_of_the_Covenant_19th-century.png/280px-Ark_of_the_Covenant_19th-century.png 2x" width="140"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ark_of_the_Covenant_19th-century.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Ezekiel" title="Ezekiel">
         Ezekiel
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Isaiah" title="Isaiah">
         Isaiah
        </a>
        had prophetic visions of the angelic Chariot and Divine Throne.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      According to adherents of Kabbalah, its origin begins with secrets that God revealed to Adam. When read by later generations of Kabbalists, the Torah's description of the creation in the Book of Genesis reveals mysteries about God himself, the true nature of
      <a href="/wiki/Adam_and_Eve" title="Adam and Eve">
       Adam and Eve
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Garden_of_Eden" title="Garden of Eden">
       Garden of Eden
      </a>
      , the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tree_of_Knowledge_of_Good_and_Evil" title="Tree of Knowledge of Good and Evil">
       Tree of Knowledge of Good and Evil
      </a>
      and the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tree_of_Life" title="Tree of Life">
       Tree of Life
      </a>
      , as well as the interaction of these supernatural entities with the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Serpent_(Bible)" title="Serpent (Bible)">
       Serpent
      </a>
      which leads to disaster when they eat the
      <a href="/wiki/Forbidden_fruit" title="Forbidden fruit">
       forbidden fruit
      </a>
      , as recorded in Genesis 3.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Artson_24-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Artson-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The Bible provides ample additional material for mythic and mystical speculation. The prophet Ezekiel's visions in particular attracted much mystical speculation, as did Isaiah's Temple vision
      <i>
       Isaiah
      </i>
      , Ch.6.
      <a href="/wiki/Jacob" title="Jacob">
       Jacob
      </a>
      's vision of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jacob%27s_Ladder_(Bible)" title="Jacob's Ladder (Bible)">
       ladder to heaven
      </a>
      provided another example of esoteric experience. Moses' encounters with the
      <a href="/wiki/Burning_bush" title="Burning bush">
       Burning bush
      </a>
      and God on Mount Sinai are evidence of mystical events in the Torah that form the origin of Jewish mystical beliefs.
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Shemhamphorasch" title="Shemhamphorasch">
       72 letter name of God
      </a>
      which is used in Jewish mysticism for meditation purposes is derived from the Hebrew verbal utterance Moses spoke in the presence of an angel, while the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_of_Reeds" title="Sea of Reeds">
       Sea of Reeds
      </a>
      parted, allowing the Hebrews to escape their approaching attackers. The miracle of the Exodus, which led to Moses receiving the
      <a href="/wiki/Ten_Commandments" title="Ten Commandments">
       Ten Commandments
      </a>
      and the Jewish Orthodox view of the acceptance of the Torah at Mount Sinai, preceded the creation of the first Jewish nation approximately three hundred years before
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saul_the_King" title="Saul the King">
       King Saul
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Mystical_doctrines_in_the_Talmudic_era">
       Mystical doctrines in the Talmudic era
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kabbalah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Mystical doctrines in the Talmudic era">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:142px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Akivakever.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="105" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Akivakever.jpg/140px-Akivakever.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Akivakever.jpg/210px-Akivakever.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Akivakever.jpg/280px-Akivakever.jpg 2x" width="140"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Akivakever.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Grave of
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rabbi_Akiva" title="Rabbi Akiva">
         Rabbi Akiva
        </a>
        in
        <a href="/wiki/Tiberias" title="Tiberias">
         Tiberias
        </a>
        . He features in Hekhalot mystical literature, and as one of the four who entered the
        <a href="/wiki/Pardes_(legend)" title="Pardes (legend)">
         Pardes
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:142px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_grave_of_Rabbi_Shimon_bar_Yochai2_(before_1899).jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1040" data-file-width="1560" height="93" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/The_grave_of_Rabbi_Shimon_bar_Yochai2_%28before_1899%29.jpg/140px-The_grave_of_Rabbi_Shimon_bar_Yochai2_%28before_1899%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/The_grave_of_Rabbi_Shimon_bar_Yochai2_%28before_1899%29.jpg/210px-The_grave_of_Rabbi_Shimon_bar_Yochai2_%28before_1899%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/The_grave_of_Rabbi_Shimon_bar_Yochai2_%28before_1899%29.jpg/280px-The_grave_of_Rabbi_Shimon_bar_Yochai2_%28before_1899%29.jpg 2x" width="140"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_grave_of_Rabbi_Shimon_bar_Yochai2_(before_1899).jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The grave of
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shimon_bar_Yochai" title="Shimon bar Yochai">
         Shimon bar Yochai
        </a>
        in
        <a href="/wiki/Meron,_Israel" title="Meron, Israel">
         Meron
        </a>
        before 1899. A Talmudic
        <a href="/wiki/Tannaim" title="Tannaim">
         Tanna
        </a>
        , he is the mystical teacher in the central Kabbalistic work, the
        <a href="/wiki/Zohar" title="Zohar">
         Zohar
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In early
      <a href="/wiki/Rabbinic_Judaism" title="Rabbinic Judaism">
       rabbinic Judaism
      </a>
      (the early centuries of the 1st millennium CE), the terms
      <i>
       Ma'aseh Bereshit
      </i>
      ("Works of Creation") and
      <i>
       Ma'aseh Merkabah
      </i>
      ("Works of the Divine Throne/Chariot") clearly indicate the
      <a href="/wiki/Midrash" title="Midrash">
       Midrashic
      </a>
      nature of these speculations; they are really based upon Genesis 1 and
      <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Ezekiel" title="Book of Ezekiel">
       Book of Ezekiel
      </a>
      1:428, while the names
      <i>
       Sitrei Torah
      </i>
      (Hidden aspects of the Torah) (Talmud
      <i>
       Hag.
      </i>
      13a) and
      <i>
       Razei Torah
      </i>
      (Torah secrets) (
      <i>
       Ab.
      </i>
      vi. 1) indicate their character as secret lore. An additional term also expanded Jewish esoteric knowledge, namely
      <i>
       Chochmah Nistara
      </i>
      (Hidden wisdom).
     </p>
     <p>
      Talmudic doctrine forbade the public teaching of esoteric doctrines and warned of their dangers. In the
      <a href="/wiki/Mishnah" title="Mishnah">
       Mishnah
      </a>
      (Hagigah 2:1), rabbis were warned to teach the mystical creation doctrines only to one student at a time.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
       <a href="#cite_note-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      To highlight the danger, in one
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jewish" title="Jewish">
       Jewish
      </a>
      aggadic ("legendary") anecdote, four prominent rabbis of the Mishnaic period (1st century CE) are said to have visited the Orchard (that is, Paradise,
      <i>
       pardes
      </i>
      , Hebrew:
      <span lang="he" xml:lang="he">
       
      </span>
      lit.,
      <i>
       orchard
      </i>
      ):
     </p>
     <blockquote class="templatequote">
      <p>
       "Four men entered
       <i>
        pardes
       </i>
       
       <a href="/wiki/Simeon_ben_Azzai" title="Simeon ben Azzai">
        Ben Azzai
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simon_ben_Zoma" title="Simon ben Zoma">
        Ben Zoma
       </a>
       ,
       <i>
        Acher
       </i>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Elisha_ben_Abuyah" title="Elisha ben Abuyah">
        Elisha ben Abuyah
       </a>
       ),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         26
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Akiba_ben_Joseph" title="Akiba ben Joseph">
        Akiba
       </a>
       . Ben Azzai looked and died; Ben Zoma looked and went mad; Acher destroyed the plants; Akiba entered in peace and departed in peace."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-27">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         27
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      In notable readings of this legend, only Rabbi Akiba was fit to handle the study of mystical doctrines. The
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Tosafot" title="Tosafot">
        Tosafot
       </a>
      </i>
      , medieval commentaries on the Talmud, say that the four sages "did not go up literally, but it appeared to them as if they went up".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
       <a href="#cite_note-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      On the other hand, Rabbi
      <a href="/wiki/Louis_Ginzberg" title="Louis Ginzberg">
       Louis Ginzberg
      </a>
      , writes in the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Jewish_Encyclopedia" title="Jewish Encyclopedia">
        Jewish Encyclopedia
       </a>
      </i>
      (19011906) that the journey to paradise "is to be taken literally and not allegorically".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
       <a href="#cite_note-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Maimonides interprets
      <i>
       pardes
      </i>
      as physics and not mysticism.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
       <a href="#cite_note-30">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">
         <span title="Quotation needed from source to verify. (March 2012)">
          need quotation to verify
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-Kabbalistic_schools">
       Pre-Kabbalistic schools
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kabbalah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Pre-Kabbalistic schools">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The mystical methods and doctrines of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hekhalot" title="Hekhalot">
       Hekhalot
      </a>
      (Heavenly "Chambers") and Merkabah (Divine "Chariot") texts, named by modern scholars from these repeated motifs, lasted from the 1st century BCE through to the 10th century, before giving way to the documented manuscript emergence of Kabbalah. Initiates were said to "descend the chariot", possibly a reference to internal introspection on the Heavenly journey through the spiritual realms. The ultimate aim was to arrive before the transcendent awe, rather than nearness, of the Divine. From the 8th to 11th centuries, the Hekhalot texts, and the proto-Kabbalistic early
      <i>
       Sefer Yetzirah
      </i>
      ("Book of Creation") made their way into European Jewish circles.
     </p>
     <p>
      Another, separate influential mystical movement, shortly before the arrival there of Kabbalistic theory, was the "
      <a href="/wiki/Chassidei_Ashkenaz" title="Chassidei Ashkenaz">
       Chassidei Ashkenaz
      </a>
      " ( ) or Medieval German Pietists from 1150-1250. This ethical-ascetic movement arose mostly among a single scholarly family, the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kalonymus_family" title="Kalonymus family">
       Kalonymus family
      </a>
      of the French and German Rhineland.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval_emergence_of_the_Kabbalah">
       Medieval emergence of the Kabbalah
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kabbalah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Medieval emergence of the Kabbalah">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nahmanides_painting.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="325" data-file-width="270" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Nahmanides_painting.jpg/120px-Nahmanides_painting.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Nahmanides_painting.jpg/180px-Nahmanides_painting.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Nahmanides_painting.jpg/240px-Nahmanides_painting.jpg 2x" width="120"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nahmanides_painting.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The 13th-century eminence of
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nachmanides" title="Nachmanides">
         Nachmanides
        </a>
        , a classic Rabbinic figure, gave Kabbalah mainstream acceptance through his Torah commentary
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Modern scholars have identified several mystical brotherhoods that functioned in Europe starting in the 12th century. Some, such as the "Iyyun Circle" and the "Unique Cherub Circle", were truly esoteric, remaining largely anonymous.
     </p>
     <p>
      There were certain
      <a href="/wiki/Rishonim" title="Rishonim">
       Rishonim
      </a>
      ("Elder Sages") of exoteric Judaism who are known to have been experts in Kabbalah. One of the best known is
      <a href="/wiki/Nahmanides" title="Nahmanides">
       Nahmanides
      </a>
      (the
      <i>
       Ramban
      </i>
      ) (11941270) whose commentary on the Torah is considered to be based on Kabbalistic knowledge. Bahya ben Asher (the
      <i>
       Rabbeinu Behaye
      </i>
      ) (d. 1340) also combined Torah commentary and Kabbalah. Another was
      <a href="/wiki/Isaac_the_Blind" title="Isaac the Blind">
       Isaac the Blind
      </a>
      (11601235), the teacher of Nahmanides, who is widely argued to have written the first work of classic Kabbalah, the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Bahir" title="Bahir">
        Bahir
       </a>
      </i>
      (Book of "Brightness").
     </p>
     <p>
      <i>
       Sefer Bahir
      </i>
      and another work, the "
      <a href="/wiki/Treatise_on_the_Left_Emanation" title="Treatise on the Left Emanation">
       Treatise on the Left Emanation
      </a>
      ", probably composed in Spain by
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Isaac_ben_Isaac_ha-Kohen&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Isaac ben Isaac ha-Kohen (page does not exist)">
       Isaac ben Isaac ha-Kohen
      </a>
      , laid the groundwork for the composition of
      <i>
       Sefer Zohar
      </i>
      ("Book of Splendor/Radiance"), written by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moses_de_Leon" title="Moses de Leon">
       Moses de Leon
      </a>
      and his mystical circle at the end of the 13th century but credited to the Talmudic sage
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shimon_bar_Yochai" title="Shimon bar Yochai">
       Shimon bar Yochai
      </a>
      (cf. Zohar). The Zohar proved to be the first truly "popular" work of Kabbalah, and the most influential. From the 13th century onward, Kabbalah began to be widely disseminated, and it branched out into an extensive literature. Historians in the 19th century, for example,
      <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Graetz" title="Heinrich Graetz">
       Heinrich Graetz
      </a>
      , argued that the emergence into public view of Jewish esotericism at this time coincides with, and represents a response to, the rising influence of the rationalist philosophy of
      <a href="/wiki/Maimonides" title="Maimonides">
       Maimonides
      </a>
      and his followers.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2013)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      Gershom Scholem sought to undermine this view as part of his resistance to seeing Kabbalah as merely a response to medieval Jewish rationalism.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2013)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      Arguing for a
      <a href="/wiki/Gnosticism" title="Gnosticism">
       gnostic
      </a>
      influence has to be seen as part of this strategy.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2013)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      More recently,
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Moshe_Idel&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Moshe Idel (page does not exist)">
       Moshe Idel
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Elliot_R._Wolfson" title="Elliot R. Wolfson">
       Elliot Wolfson
      </a>
      have independently argued that the impact of Maimonides can be seen in the change from orality to writing in the 13th century.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2013)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      That is, Kabbalists committed to writing many of their oral traditions in part as a response to the attempt of Maimonides to explain the older esoteric subjects philosophically.
     </p>
     <p>
      Many Orthodox Jews reject the idea that Kabbalah underwent significant historical development or change such as has been proposed above. After the composition known as the Zohar was presented to the public in the 13th century, the term "Kabbalah" began to refer more specifically to teachings derived from, or related to, the
      <i>
       Zohar
      </i>
      . At an even later time, the term began to generally be applied to Zoharic teachings as elaborated upon by Isaac Luria Arizal. Historians generally date the start of Kabbalah as a major influence in Jewish thought and practice with the publication of the Zohar and climaxing with the spread of the Arizal's teachings. The majority of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haredi" title="Haredi">
       Haredi
      </a>
      Jews accept the Zohar as the representative of the
      <i>
       Ma'aseh Merkavah
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       Ma'aseh B'reshit
      </i>
      that are referred to in Talmudic texts.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
       <a href="#cite_note-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Early_modern_era:_Lurianic_Kabbalah">
       Early modern era: Lurianic Kabbalah
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kabbalah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Early modern era: Lurianic Kabbalah">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SafedDSCN4077.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3648" data-file-width="2736" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/SafedDSCN4077.JPG/120px-SafedDSCN4077.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/SafedDSCN4077.JPG/180px-SafedDSCN4077.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/SafedDSCN4077.JPG/240px-SafedDSCN4077.JPG 2x" width="120"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SafedDSCN4077.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The leading scholars in 16th-century Safed invigorated mainstream Judaism through new legal, liturgical, exegetical and Lurianic-mythological developments
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Following the upheavals and dislocations in the Jewish world as a result of
      <a href="/wiki/Anti-Judaism" title="Anti-Judaism">
       anti-Judaism
      </a>
      during the
      <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">
       Middle Ages
      </a>
      , and the national trauma of the
      <a href="/wiki/Alhambra_Decree" title="Alhambra Decree">
       expulsion from Spain
      </a>
      in 1492, closing the Spanish Jewish flowering, Jews began to search for signs of when the long-awaited
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jewish_Messiah" title="Jewish Messiah">
       Jewish Messiah
      </a>
      would come to comfort them in their painful exiles. In the 1500s the community of Safed in the Galilee became the centre of Jewish mystical, exegetical, legal and liturgical developments. The Safed mystics responded to the Spanish expulsion by turning Kabbalistic doctrine and practice towards a messianic focus.
      <a href="/wiki/Moses_ben_Jacob_Cordovero" title="Moses ben Jacob Cordovero">
       Moses Cordovero
      </a>
      and his school popularized the teachings of the Zohar which had until then been only a restricted work. Cordovero's comprehensive works achieved the systemisation of preceding Kabbalah. The author of the
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shulkhan_Arukh" title="Shulkhan Arukh">
        Shulkhan Arukh
       </a>
      </i>
      (the normative Jewish "Code of Law"), Rabbi
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yosef_Karo" title="Yosef Karo">
       Yosef Karo
      </a>
      (14881575), was also a scholar of Kabbalah who kept a personal mystical diary.
      <a href="/wiki/Moshe_Alshich" title="Moshe Alshich">
       Moshe Alshich
      </a>
      wrote a mystical commentary on the Torah, and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shlomo_Alkabetz" title="Shlomo Alkabetz">
       Shlomo Alkabetz
      </a>
      wrote Kabbalistic commentaries and poems.
     </p>
     <p>
      The messianism of the Safed mystics culminated in Kabbalah receiving its biggest transformation in the Jewish world with the explication of its new interpretation from Rabbi Isaac Luria (15341572), by his disciples Hayim Vital and
      <a href="/wiki/Israel_Sarug" title="Israel Sarug">
       Israel Sarug
      </a>
      . Both transcribed Luria's teachings (in variant forms) gaining them widespread popularity, Sarug taking Lurianic Kabbalah to Europe, Vital authoring the latterly canonical version. Luria's teachings came to rival the influence of the Zohar and Luria stands, alongside Moses de Leon, as the most influential mystic in Jewish history.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Ban_on_studying_Kabbalah">
       Ban on studying Kabbalah
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kabbalah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Ban on studying Kabbalah">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:246px; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #c6dbf7;">
      <div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
       <div>
        <p>
         "I have seen it written that the prohibition from Above to refrain from open study in the wisdom of truth was only for a limited period, until the end of 1490, but from then on the prohibition has been lifted and permission was granted to study the Zohar. Since 1540 it has been a great Mitzva (commandment) for the masses to study in public, old and young... and that is because the Messiah will come because of that and not because of any other reason. Therefore, we must not be negligent."
        </p>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div style="text-align: left;">
       Rabbi Abraham Ben Mordechai Azulai, Introduction to the book, Ohr HaChama [Light of the Sun]
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         32
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The ban on studying Kabbalah was lifted by the efforts of the 16th-century kabbalist Rabbi
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Avraham_Azulai" title="Avraham Azulai">
       Avraham Azulai
      </a>
      (15701643).
     </p>
     <blockquote class="templatequote">
      <p>
       "I have found it written that all that has been decreed Above forbidding open involvement in the Wisdom of Truth [Kabbalah] was [only meant for] the limited time period until the year 5,250 (1490 C.E.). From then on after is called the "Last Generation", and what was forbidden is [now] allowed. And permission is granted to occupy ourselves in the [study of] Zohar. And from the year 5,300 (1540 C.E.) it is most desirable that the masses both those great and small [in Torah], should occupy themselves [in the study of Kabbalah], as it says in the Raya M'hemna [a section of the Zohar]. And because in this merit King Mashiach will come in the futureand not in any other meritit is not proper to be discouraged [from the study of Kabbalah]."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
        <a href="#cite_note-33">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         33
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      The question, however, is whether the ban ever existed in the first place.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words">
         <span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (November 2013)">
          according to whom?
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      Concerning the above quote by Avraham Azulai, it has found many versions in English, another is this
     </p>
     <blockquote class="templatequote">
      <p>
       "From the year 1540 and onward, the basic levels of Kabbalah must be taught publicly to everyone, young and old. Only through Kabbalah will we forever eliminate war, destruction, and man's inhumanity to his fellow man."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
        <a href="#cite_note-34">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         34
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      The lines concerning the year 1490 are also missing from the Hebrew edition of
      <i>
       Hesed L'Avraham
      </i>
      , the source work that both of these quote from. Furthermore, by Azulai's view the ban was lifted thirty years before his birth, a time that would have corresponded with Haim Vital's publication of the teaching of Isaac Luria. Moshe Isserles understood there to be only a minor restriction, in his words, "One's belly must be full of meat and wine, discerning between the prohibited and the permitted."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
       <a href="#cite_note-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He is supported by the Bier Hetiv, the Pithei Teshuva as well as the
      <a href="/wiki/Vilna_Gaon" title="Vilna Gaon">
       Vilna Gaon
      </a>
      . The Vilna Gaon says, "There was never any ban or enactment restricting the study of the wisdom of Kabbalah. Any who says there is has never studied Kabbalah, has never seen PaRDeS, and speaks as an ignoramus."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
       <a href="#cite_note-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Sefardi_and_Mizrahi">
       Sefardi and Mizrahi
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kabbalah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Sefardi and Mizrahi">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:72px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:YeshivatBeitEl_entree.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2093" data-file-width="869" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/YeshivatBeitEl_entree.jpg/70px-YeshivatBeitEl_entree.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/YeshivatBeitEl_entree.jpg/105px-YeshivatBeitEl_entree.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/YeshivatBeitEl_entree.jpg/140px-YeshivatBeitEl_entree.jpg 2x" width="70"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:YeshivatBeitEl_entree.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Beit_El_Synagogue" title="Beit El Synagogue">
         Synagogue Beit El
        </a>
        Jerusalem. Oriental Judaism has its own chain of Kabbalah
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The Kabbalah of the
      <a href="/wiki/Sephardi_Jews" title="Sephardi Jews">
       Sefardi
      </a>
      (Portuguese or Spanish) and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mizrahi" title="Mizrahi">
       Mizrahi
      </a>
      (Middle East, North Africa, and the Caucasus) Torah scholars has a long history. Kabbalah in various forms was widely studied, commented upon, and expanded by North African, Turkish, Yemenite, and Asian scholars from the 16th century onward. It flourished among Sefardic Jews in Tzfat (Safed), Israel even before the arrival of Isaac Luria. Yosef Karo, author of the
      <i>
       Shulchan Arukh
      </i>
      was part of the Tzfat school of Kabbalah. Shlomo Alkabetz, author of the hymn
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Lekhah_Dodi" title="Lekhah Dodi">
        Lekhah Dodi
       </a>
      </i>
      , taught there.
     </p>
     <p>
      His disciple Moses ben Jacob Cordovero authored
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Pardes_Rimonim" title="Pardes Rimonim">
        Pardes Rimonim
       </a>
      </i>
      , an organised, exhaustive compilation of kabbalistic teachings on a variety of subjects up to that point. Cordovero headed the academy of Tzfat until his death, when Isaac Luria rose to prominence. Rabbi Moshe's disciple
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eliyahu_De_Vidas" title="Eliyahu De Vidas">
       Eliyahu De Vidas
      </a>
      authored the classic work,
      <i>
       Reishit Chochma
      </i>
      , combining kabbalistic and
      <i>
       mussar
      </i>
      (moral) teachings.
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chaim_Vital" title="Chaim Vital">
       Chaim Vital
      </a>
      also studied under Cordovero, but with the arrival of Luria became his main disciple. Vital claimed to be the only one authorised to transmit the Ari's teachings, though other disciples also published books presenting Luria's teachings.
     </p>
     <p>
      The Oriental Kabbalist tradition continues until today among Sephardi and Mizrachi Hakham sages and study circles. Among leading figures were the Yemenite
      <a href="/wiki/Shalom_Sharabi" title="Shalom Sharabi">
       Shalom Sharabi
      </a>
      (17201777) of the
      <a href="/wiki/Beit_El_Synagogue" title="Beit El Synagogue">
       Beit El Synagogue
      </a>
      , the Jerusalemite
      <a href="/wiki/Chaim_Joseph_David_Azulai" title="Chaim Joseph David Azulai">
       Hida
      </a>
      (17241806), the Baghdad leader
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ben_Ish_Chai" title="Ben Ish Chai">
       Ben Ish Chai
      </a>
      (18321909), and the
      <a href="/wiki/Baba_Sali" title="Baba Sali">
       Abuhatzeira
      </a>
      dynasty.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Maharal">
       <i>
        Maharal
       </i>
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kabbalah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Maharal">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Loew-rabin-tombstone.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3872" data-file-width="2592" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Loew-rabin-tombstone.jpg/120px-Loew-rabin-tombstone.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Loew-rabin-tombstone.jpg/180px-Loew-rabin-tombstone.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Loew-rabin-tombstone.jpg/240px-Loew-rabin-tombstone.jpg 2x" width="120"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Loew-rabin-tombstone.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The 16th-century
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maharal" title="Maharal">
         Maharal
        </a>
        of Prague articulated a mystical exegesis in philosophical language
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      One of the most innovative theologians in early-modern Judaism was
      <a href="/wiki/Judah_Loew_ben_Bezalel" title="Judah Loew ben Bezalel">
       Judah Loew ben Bezalel
      </a>
      (15251609) known as the "Maharal of Prague". Many of his written works survive and are studied for their unusual combination of the mystical and philosophical approaches in Judaism. While conversant in Kabbalistic learning, he expresses Jewish mystical thought in his own individual approach without reference to Kabbalistic terms.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
       <a href="#cite_note-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Maharal is most well known in popular culture for the legend of the
      <a href="/wiki/Golem" title="Golem">
       golem
      </a>
      of Prague, associated with him in folklore. However, his thought influenced Hasidism, for example being studied in the introspective Przysucha school. During the 20th century,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isaac_Hutner" title="Isaac Hutner">
       Isaac Hutner
      </a>
      (19061980) continued to spread the Maharal's works indirectly through his own teachings and publications within the non-Hasidic yeshiva world.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Sabbatian_mysticism">
       Sabbatian mysticism
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kabbalah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Sabbatian mysticism">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      The spiritual and mystical yearnings of many Jews remained frustrated after the death of Isaac Luria and his disciples and colleagues. No hope was in sight for many following the devastation and mass killings of the
      <a href="/wiki/Pogrom" title="Pogrom">
       pogroms
      </a>
      that followed in the wake of the
      <a href="/wiki/Khmelnytsky_Uprising" title="Khmelnytsky Uprising">
       Chmielnicki Uprising
      </a>
      (16481654), the largest single massacre of Jews until the Holocaust, and it was at this time that a controversial scholar by the name of
      <a href="/wiki/Sabbatai_Zevi" title="Sabbatai Zevi">
       Sabbatai Zevi
      </a>
      (16261676) captured the hearts and minds of the Jewish masses of that time with the promise of a newly minted messianic
      <a href="/wiki/Millennialism" title="Millennialism">
       Millennialism
      </a>
      in the form of his own personage.
     </p>
     <p>
      His charisma, mystical teachings that included repeated pronunciations of the holy
      <a href="/wiki/Tetragrammaton" title="Tetragrammaton">
       Tetragrammaton
      </a>
      in public, tied to an unstable personality, and with the help of his greatest enthusiast,
      <a href="/wiki/Nathan_of_Gaza" title="Nathan of Gaza">
       Nathan of Gaza
      </a>
      , convinced the Jewish masses that the Jewish Messiah had finally come. It seemed that the esoteric teachings of Kabbalah had found their "champion" and had triumphed, but this era of Jewish history unravelled when Zevi became an
      <a href="/wiki/Apostasy" title="Apostasy">
       apostate
      </a>
      to Judaism by converting to Islam after he was arrested by the Ottoman Sultan and threatened with execution for attempting a plan to conquer the world and rebuild the
      <a href="/wiki/Temple_in_Jerusalem" title="Temple in Jerusalem">
       Temple in Jerusalem
      </a>
      . Unwilling to give up their messianic expectations, a minority of Zvi's Jewish followers converted to Islam along with him.
     </p>
     <p>
      Many of his followers, known as
      <a href="/wiki/Sabbateans" title="Sabbateans">
       Sabbatians
      </a>
      , continued to worship him in secret, explaining his conversion not as an effort to save his life but to recover the sparks of the holy in each religion, and most leading rabbis were always on guard to root them out. The
      <a href="/wiki/D%C3%B6nmeh" title="Dnmeh">
       Dnmeh
      </a>
      movement in modern Turkey is a surviving remnant of the Sabbatian schism.
     </p>
     <p>
      Due to the chaos caused in the Jewish world, the Rabbinic prohibition against studying Kabbalah established itself firmly within the Jewish religion. One of the conditions allowing a man to study and engage himself in the Kabbalah was to be at least forty years old. This age requirement came about during this period and is not Talmudic in origin but Rabbinic. Many Jews are familiar with this ruling, but are not aware of its origins.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
       <a href="#cite_note-38">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        38
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Moreover, the prohibition is not halakhic in nature. According to Moses Cordovero, halakhically, one must be of age twenty to engage in the Kabbalah. Many famous kabbalists, including the ARI, Rabbi Nachman of Breslov, Yehuda Ashlag, were younger than twenty when they began.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Frankism">
       Frankism
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kabbalah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Frankism">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      The Sabbatian movement was followed by that of the
      <a href="/wiki/Frankism" title="Frankism">
       Frankists
      </a>
      who were disciples of
      <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Frank" title="Jacob Frank">
       Jacob Frank
      </a>
      (17261791) who eventually became an apostate to Judaism by apparently converting to
      <a href="/wiki/Catholicism" title="Catholicism">
       Catholicism
      </a>
      . This era of disappointment did not stem the Jewish masses' yearnings for "mystical" leadership.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Modern-era_traditional_Kabbalah">
       Modern-era traditional Kabbalah
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kabbalah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Modern-era traditional Kabbalah">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:142px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Headstone_of_Moshe_Chaim_Luzzatto_in_Tiberias.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="105" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Headstone_of_Moshe_Chaim_Luzzatto_in_Tiberias.jpg/140px-Headstone_of_Moshe_Chaim_Luzzatto_in_Tiberias.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Headstone_of_Moshe_Chaim_Luzzatto_in_Tiberias.jpg/210px-Headstone_of_Moshe_Chaim_Luzzatto_in_Tiberias.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Headstone_of_Moshe_Chaim_Luzzatto_in_Tiberias.jpg/280px-Headstone_of_Moshe_Chaim_Luzzatto_in_Tiberias.jpg 2x" width="140"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Headstone_of_Moshe_Chaim_Luzzatto_in_Tiberias.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Moshe Chaim Luzzatto, a leading Italian kabbalist, also wrote secular works, which the Haskalah see as the start of modern
        <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_literature" title="Hebrew literature">
         Hebrew literature
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:117px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vilna_Gaon_authentic_portrait.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="747" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Vilna_Gaon_authentic_portrait.JPG/115px-Vilna_Gaon_authentic_portrait.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Vilna_Gaon_authentic_portrait.JPG/173px-Vilna_Gaon_authentic_portrait.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Vilna_Gaon_authentic_portrait.JPG/230px-Vilna_Gaon_authentic_portrait.JPG 2x" width="115"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vilna_Gaon_authentic_portrait.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/Vilna_Gaon" title="Vilna Gaon">
         Vilna Gaon
        </a>
        , 18th-century leader of Rabbinic opposition to Hasidism - a Kabbalist who opposed Hasidic doctrinal and practical innovations
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Rabbi
      <a href="/wiki/Moshe_Chaim_Luzzatto" title="Moshe Chaim Luzzatto">
       Moshe Chaim Luzzatto
      </a>
      (17071746), based in Italy, was a precocious Talmudic scholar who deduced a need for the public teaching and study of Kabbalah. He established a yeshiva for Kabbalah study and actively recruited students. He wrote copious manuscripts in an appealing clear Hebrew style, all of which gained the attention of both admirers and rabbinical critics, who feared another "Shabbetai Zevi (false messiah) in the making".
