His rabbinical opponents forced him to close his school, hand over and destroy many of his most precious unpublished kabbalistic writings, and go into exile in the Netherlands. He eventually moved to the
      <a href="/wiki/Land_of_Israel" title="Land of Israel">
       Land of Israel
      </a>
      . Some of his most important works, such as
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Derekh_Hashem" title="Derekh Hashem">
        Derekh Hashem
       </a>
      </i>
      , survive and are used
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch">
         <span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (January 2014)">
          by whom?
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      as a gateway to the world of Jewish mysticism.
     </p>
     <p>
      Rabbi Elijah of Vilna (Vilna Gaon) (17201797), based in Lithuania, had his teachings encoded and publicised by his disciples, such as Rabbi
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chaim_Volozhin" title="Chaim Volozhin">
       Chaim Volozhin
      </a>
      , who (poshumously) published the mystical-ethical work
      <i>
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Nefesh_HaChaim&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Nefesh HaChaim (page does not exist)">
        Nefesh HaChaim
       </a>
      </i>
      <a class="external autonumber" href="http://www.amazon.com/dp/061569991X" rel="nofollow">
       [4]
      </a>
      . He staunchly opposed the new Hasidic movement and warned against their public displays of religious fervour inspired by the mystical teachings of their rabbis. Although the Vilna Gaon did not look with favor on the Hasidic movement, he did not prohibit the study and engagement in the Kabbalah. This is evident from his writings in the
      <i>
       Even Shlema
      </i>
      . "He that is able to understand secrets of the Torah and does not try to understand them will be judged harshly, may God have mercy". (The Vilna Gaon,
      <i>
       Even Shlema
      </i>
      , 8:24). "The Redemption will only come about through learning Torah, and the essence of the Redemption depends upon learning Kabbalah" (The Vilna Gaon,
      <i>
       Even Shlema
      </i>
      , 11:3).
     </p>
     <p>
      In the Oriental tradition of Kabbalah, Shalom Sharabi (17201777) from Yemen was a major esoteric clarifier of the works of the Ari. The Beit El Synagogue, "yeshivah of the kabbalists", which he came to head, was one of the few communities to bring Lurianic meditation into communal prayer.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nola.com_9-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-nola.com-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
       <a href="#cite_note-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In the 20th century, Yehuda Ashlag (18851954) in Mandate Palestine became a leading esoteric kabbalist in the traditional mode, who translated the Zohar into Hebrew with a new approach in Lurianic Kabbalah.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Hasidic_Judaism">
       Hasidic Judaism
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kabbalah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Hasidic Judaism">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Besht_Shul2_Medzhibozh.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="591" data-file-width="774" height="115" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Besht_Shul2_Medzhibozh.jpg/150px-Besht_Shul2_Medzhibozh.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Besht_Shul2_Medzhibozh.jpg/225px-Besht_Shul2_Medzhibozh.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Besht_Shul2_Medzhibozh.jpg/300px-Besht_Shul2_Medzhibozh.jpg 2x" width="150"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Besht_Shul2_Medzhibozh.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Synagogue of the
        <a href="/wiki/Baal_Shem_Tov" title="Baal Shem Tov">
         Baal Shem Tov
        </a>
        , founder of Hasidism, in
        <a href="/wiki/Medzhybizh" title="Medzhybizh">
         Medzhybizh
        </a>
        Ukraine. It gave a new phase to Jewish mysticism, seeking its popularisation through
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deveikut" title="Deveikut">
         internal
        </a>
        correspondence
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Rabbi Israel ben Eliezer Baal Shem Tov (16981760), founder of Hasidism in the area of the Ukraine, spread teachings based on Lurianic Kabbalah, but adapted to a different aim of immediate psychological perception of Divine Omnipresence amidst the mundane. The emotional, ecstatic fervour of early Hasidism developed from previous
      <a href="/wiki/Tzadikim_Nistarim" title="Tzadikim Nistarim">
       Nistarim
      </a>
      circles of mystical activity, but instead sought communal revival of the common folk by reframing Judaism around the central principle of
      <a href="/wiki/Devekut" title="Devekut">
       devekut
      </a>
      (mystical cleaving to God) for all. This new approach turned formerly esoteric elite kabbalistic theory into a popular social mysticism movement for the first time, with its own doctrines, classic texts, teachings and customs. From the Baal Shem Tov sprang the wide ongoing schools of Hasidic Judaism, each with different approaches and thought. Hasidism instituted a new concept of
      <a href="/wiki/Tzadik" title="Tzadik">
       Tzadik
      </a>
      leadership in Jewish mysticism, where the elite scholars of mystical texts now took on a social role as embodiments and intercessors of Divinity for the masses. With the 19th century consolidation of the movement,
      <a href="/wiki/Rebbe" title="Rebbe">
       leadership
      </a>
      became dynastic. Among later Hasidic schools:
     </p>
     <p>
      Rebbe Nachman of Breslov (17721810), the great-grandson of the Baal Shem Tov, revitalised and further expanded the latter's teachings, amassing a following of thousands in Ukraine, Belarus, Lithuania and Poland. In a unique amalgam of Hasidic and
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mitnagdim" title="Mitnagdim">
        Mitnagid
       </a>
      </i>
      approaches, Rebbe Nachman emphasised study of both Kabbalah and serious Torah scholarship to his disciples. His teachings also differed from the way other Hasidic groups were developing, as he rejected the idea of hereditary
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hasidic_dynasty" title="Hasidic dynasty">
       Hasidic dynasties
      </a>
      and taught that each Hasid must "search for the
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tzaddik" title="Tzaddik">
        tzaddik
       </a>
      </i>
      ('saintly/righteous person')" for himself and within himself.
     </p>
     <p>
      The Habad-Lubavitch intellectual school of Hasidism broke away from General-Hasidism's emotional faith orientation, by making the mind central as the route to the internal heart. Its texts combine what they view as rational investigation with explanation of Kabbalah through articulating unity in a common Divine essence. In recent times, the messianic element latent in Hasidism has come to the fore in Habad.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century_influence">
       20th century influence
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kabbalah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: 20th century influence">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Jewish mysticism has influenced the thought of some major Jewish theologians in the 20th century, outside of Kabbalistic or Hasidic traditions. The first Chief Rabbi of Mandate Palestine,
      <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Isaac_Kook" title="Abraham Isaac Kook">
       Abraham Isaac Kook
      </a>
      was a mystical thinker who drew heavily on Kabbalistic notions through his own poetic terminology. His writings are concerned with fusing the false divisions between sacred and secular, rational and mystical, legal and imaginative. Students of
      <a href="/wiki/Joseph_B._Soloveitchik" title="Joseph B. Soloveitchik">
       Joseph B. Soloveitchik
      </a>
      , figurehead of American
      <a href="/wiki/Modern_Orthodox_Judaism" title="Modern Orthodox Judaism">
       Modern Orthodox Judaism
      </a>
      have read the influence of Kabbalistic symbols in his philosophical works.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
       <a href="#cite_note-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Neo-Hasidism, rather than Kabbalah, shaped
      <a href="/wiki/Martin_Buber" title="Martin Buber">
       Martin Buber
      </a>
      's philosophy of dialogue and
      <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Joshua_Heschel" title="Abraham Joshua Heschel">
       Abraham Joshua Heschel
      </a>
      's
      <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Judaism" title="Conservative Judaism">
       Conservative Judaism
      </a>
      . Lurianic symbols of Tzimtzum and Shevirah have informed
      <a href="/wiki/Holocaust_theology" title="Holocaust theology">
       Holocaust theologians
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
       <a href="#cite_note-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        41
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Concepts">
       Concepts
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kabbalah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Concepts">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Concealed_and_Revealed_God">
       Concealed and Revealed God
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kabbalah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Concealed and Revealed God">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:137px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ein_sof.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="376" data-file-width="376" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/15/Ein_sof.png/135px-Ein_sof.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/15/Ein_sof.png/203px-Ein_sof.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/15/Ein_sof.png/270px-Ein_sof.png 2x" width="135"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ein_sof.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Metaphorical scheme of emanated spiritual worlds within the
        <a href="/wiki/Ein_Sof" title="Ein Sof">
         Ein Sof
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The nature of the Divine prompted kabbalists to envision two aspects to God: (a) God in essence, absolutely transcendent, unknowable, limitless
      <a href="/wiki/Divine_simplicity" title="Divine simplicity">
       Divine simplicity
      </a>
      , and (b) God in manifestation, the revealed persona of God through which He creates and sustains and relates to mankind. Kabbalists speak of the first as
      <i>
       Ein/Ayn Sof
      </i>
      (  "the infinite/endless", literally "that which has no limits"). Of the impersonal Ein Sof nothing can be grasped. The second aspect of Divine emanations, however, are accessible to human perception, dynamically interacting throughout spiritual and physical existence, reveal the Divine immanently, and are bound up in the life of man. Kabbalists believe that these two aspects are not contradictory but complement one another, emanations revealing the concealed mystery from within the Godhead.
     </p>
     <p>
      The Zohar reads the first words of Genesis
      <i>
       BeReishit Bara Elohim In the beginning God created
      </i>
      as "With the level of "
      <i>
       Reishit-Beginning
      </i>
      " the Ein Sof created
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Elohim" title="Elohim">
        Elohim
       </a>
      </i>
      -God's manifestation in Creation
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify">
         <span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (October 2014)">
          clarification needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      :
     </p>
     <blockquote class="templatequote">
      <p>
       "At the very beginning the King made engravings in the supernal purity. A spark of blackness emerged in the sealed within the sealed, from the mystery of the Ayn Sof, a mist within matter, implanted in a ring, no white, no black, no red, no yellow, no colour at all. When He measured with the standard of measure, He made colours to provide light. Within the spark, in the innermost part, emerged a source, from which the colours are painted below; it is sealed among the sealed things of the mystery of Ayn Sof. It penetrated, yet did not penetrate its air. It was not known at all until, from the pressure of its penetration, a single point shone, sealed, supernal. Beyond this point nothing is known, so it is called
       <i>
        reishit
       </i>
       (beginning): the first word of all..."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
        <a href="#cite_note-42">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         42
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       "
      </p>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      The structure of emanations has been described in various ways:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sefirot" title="Sefirot">
       Sefirot
      </a>
      (Divine attributes) and
      <a href="/wiki/Partzufim" title="Partzufim">
       Partzufim
      </a>
      (Divine "faces"),
      <a href="/wiki/Ohr" title="Ohr">
       Ohr
      </a>
      (spiritual light and flow), Names of God and the supernal Torah, Olamot (Spiritual Worlds), a Divine Tree and Archetypal Man, Angelic Chariot and Palaces, male and female, enclothed layers of reality, inwardly holy vitality and external Kelipot shells, 613 channels ("limbs" of the King) and the Divine souls in man. These symbols are used to describe various parts and aspects of the model.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Sefirot_and_the_Divine_Feminine">
       Sefirot and the Divine Feminine
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kabbalah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Sefirot and the Divine Feminine">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main articles:
      <a href="/wiki/Sephirot" title="Sephirot">
       Sephirot
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Shekhinah" title="Shekhinah">
       Shekhinah
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:92px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sefiroticky_strom.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="699" data-file-width="340" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Sefiroticky_strom.jpg/90px-Sefiroticky_strom.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Sefiroticky_strom.jpg/135px-Sefiroticky_strom.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Sefiroticky_strom.jpg/180px-Sefiroticky_strom.jpg 2x" width="90"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sefiroticky_strom.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Scheme of descending
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sefirot" title="Sefirot">
         Sefirot
        </a>
        in 3 columns, as a tree with roots above and branches below
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Sephirot" title="Sephirot">
        Sephirot
       </a>
      </i>
      (also spelled "sephiroth") (singular
      <i>
       sefirah
      </i>
      ) are the ten emanations and attributes of
      <a href="/wiki/God_in_Judaism" title="God in Judaism">
       God
      </a>
      with which he continually sustains the universe in existence. The Zohar and other formative texts elaborate on their emergence from concealment and potential in the infinite unity of the Ein Sof. Cordovero systemises them as one light poured into ten created vessels. Comparison of his counting with Luria's, describes dual rational and unconscious aspects of Kabbalah. Two metaphors are used to describe the
      <i>
       sephirot
      </i>
      , their
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theocentric" title="Theocentric">
       theocentric
      </a>
      manifestation as the Trees of Life and Knowledge, and their
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anthropocentric" title="Anthropocentric">
       anthropocentric
      </a>
      correspondence in man, exemplified as
      <a href="/wiki/Adam_Kadmon" title="Adam Kadmon">
       Adam Kadmon
      </a>
      . This dual-directional perspective embodies the cyclical, inclusive nature of the divine flow, where alternative divine and human perspectives have validity. The central metaphor of man allows human understanding of the sephirot, as they correspond to the psychological faculties of the soul, and incorporate masculine and feminine aspects after Genesis 1:27 ("God created man in His own image, in the image of God He created him, male and female He created them"). Corresponding to the last
      <i>
       sefirah
      </i>
      in Creation is the indwelling shekhinah (Feminine Divine Presence). Downward flow of divine Light in Creation forms the supernal
      <a href="/wiki/Four_Worlds" title="Four Worlds">
       Four Worlds
      </a>
      ;
      <a href="/wiki/Atziluth" title="Atziluth">
       Atziluth
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Beri%27ah" title="Beri'ah">
       Beri'ah
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Yetzirah" title="Yetzirah">
       Yetzirah
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Assiah" title="Assiah">
       Assiah
      </a>
      manifesting the dominance of successive sephirot towards action in this world. The acts of man unite or divide the Heavenly masculine and feminine aspects of the sephirot, their anthropomorphic harmony completing Creation. As the spiritual foundation of Creation, the sephirot correspond to the
      <a href="/wiki/Names_of_God_in_Judaism" title="Names of God in Judaism">
       names of God in Judaism
      </a>
      and the particular nature of any entity.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Ten_Sefirot_as_process_of_Creation">
       Ten Sefirot as process of Creation
       <span id="Ten_Sephirot_as_process_of_Creation">
       </span>
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kabbalah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Ten Sefirot as process of Creation">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      According to Lurianic cosmology, the
      <i>
       sefirot
      </i>
      correspond to various levels of creation (ten
      <i>
       sefirot
      </i>
      in each of the Four Worlds, and four worlds within each of the larger four worlds, each containing ten
      <i>
       sefirot
      </i>
      , which themselves contain ten
      <i>
       sefirot
      </i>
      , to an infinite number of possibilities),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
       <a href="#cite_note-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and are emanated from the Creator for the purpose of creating the universe. The
      <i>
       sefirot
      </i>
      are considered revelations of the Creator's will (
      <i>
       ratzon
      </i>
      ),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
       <a href="#cite_note-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and they should not be understood as ten different "gods" but as ten different ways the one God reveals his will through the Emanations. It is not God who changes but the ability to perceive God that changes.
     </p>
     <p>
      Altogether, eleven
      <i>
       sefirot
      </i>
      are named. However
      <i>
       Keter
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       Daat
      </i>
      are unconscious and conscious dimensions of one principle, conserving 10 forces. The names of the
      <i>
       sefirot
      </i>
      in descending order are:
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Keter_(Kabbalah)" title="Keter (Kabbalah)">
         Keter
        </a>
       </i>
       (supernal crown, representing above-conscious will)
      </li>
      <li>
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Chokhmah_(Kabbalah)" title="Chokhmah (Kabbalah)">
         Chochmah
        </a>
       </i>
       (the highest potential of thought)
      </li>
      <li>
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Binah_(Kabbalah)" title="Binah (Kabbalah)">
         Binah
        </a>
       </i>
       (the understanding of the potential)
      </li>
      <li>
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Da%27at" title="Da'at">
         Daat
        </a>
       </i>
       (intellect of knowledge)
      </li>
      <li>
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chesed_(Kabbalah)" title="Chesed (Kabbalah)">
         Chesed
        </a>
       </i>
       (sometimes referred to as
       <i>
        Gedolah
       </i>
       -greatness) (loving-kindness)
      </li>
      <li>
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gevurah_(Kabbalah)" title="Gevurah (Kabbalah)">
         Gevurah
        </a>
       </i>
       (sometimes referred to as
       <i>
        Din
       </i>
       -justice or
       <i>
        Pachad
       </i>
       -fear) (severity/strength)
      </li>
      <li>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tiphereth_(Kabbalah)" title="Tiphereth (Kabbalah)">
        Rachamim also known as
        <i>
         Tiphereth
        </i>
       </a>
       (mercy)
      </li>
      <li>
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Netzach_(Kabbalah)" title="Netzach (Kabbalah)">
         Netzach
        </a>
       </i>
       (victory/eternity)
      </li>
      <li>
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Hod_(Kabbalah)" title="Hod (Kabbalah)">
         Hod
        </a>
       </i>
       (glory/splendour)
      </li>
      <li>
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yesod_(Kabbalah)" title="Yesod (Kabbalah)">
         Yesod
        </a>
       </i>
       (foundation)
      </li>
      <li>
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Malkuth" title="Malkuth">
         Malkuth
        </a>
       </i>
       (kingdom)
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Ten_Sefirot_as_process_of_ethics">
       Ten Sefirot as process of ethics
       <span id="Ten_Sephirot_as_process_of_ethics">
       </span>
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kabbalah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Ten Sefirot as process of ethics">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:137px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shefa_Tal.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="381" data-file-width="450" height="114" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Shefa_Tal.png/135px-Shefa_Tal.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Shefa_Tal.png/203px-Shefa_Tal.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Shefa_Tal.png/270px-Shefa_Tal.png 2x" width="135"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shefa_Tal.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        In the 16-17th centuries Kabbalah was popularised through a new genre of ethical literature, related to Kabbalistic meditation
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Divine creation by means of the Ten Sefirot is an ethical process. They represent the different aspects of Morality. Loving-Kindness is a possible moral justification found in Chessed, and Gevurah is the Moral Justification of Justice and both are mediated by Mercy which is Rachamim. However, these pillars of morality become immoral once they become extremes. When Loving-Kindness become extreme it can lead to sexual depravity and lack of Justice to the wicked. When Justice becomes extreme, it can lead to torture and the Murder of innocents and unfair punishment.
     </p>
     <p>
      "Righteous" humans (
      <i>
       tzadikim
      </i>
      ) ascend these ethical qualities of the ten
      <i>
       sefirot
      </i>
      by doing righteous actions. If there were no righteous humans, the blessings of God would become completely hidden, and creation would cease to exist. While real human actions are the "Foundation" (
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Yesod" title="Yesod">
        Yesod
       </a>
      </i>
      ) of this universe (
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malchut" title="Malchut">
        Malchut
       </a>
      </i>
      ), these actions must accompany the conscious intention of compassion. Compassionate actions are often impossible without faith (
      <i>
       Emunah
      </i>
      ), meaning to trust that God always supports compassionate actions even when God seems hidden. Ultimately, it is necessary to show compassion toward oneself too in order to share compassion toward others. This "selfish" enjoyment of God's blessings but only in order to empower oneself to assist others is an important aspect of "Restriction", and is considered a kind of
      <a href="/wiki/Golden_mean_(philosophy)" title="Golden mean (philosophy)">
       golden mean
      </a>
      in kabbalah, corresponding to the
      <i>
       sefirah
      </i>
      of Adornment (
      <a href="/wiki/Tiferet" title="Tiferet">
       Tiferet
      </a>
      ) being part of the "Middle Column".
     </p>
     <p>
      Moses ben Jacob Cordovero, wrote
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Tomer_Devorah" title="Tomer Devorah">
        Tomer Devorah
       </a>
      </i>
      (
      <i>
       Palm Tree of Deborah
      </i>
      ), he presents an ethical teaching of Judaism in the kabbalistic context of the ten
      <i>
       sefirot
      </i>
      .
      <i>
       Tomer Devorah
      </i>
      has become also a foundational
      <a href="/wiki/Musar_literature" title="Musar literature">
       Musar text
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
       <a href="#cite_note-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Descending_spiritual_Worlds">
       Descending spiritual Worlds
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kabbalah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Descending spiritual Worlds">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Medieval Kabbalists believed that all things are linked to God through these emanations, making all levels in creation part of one great, gradually descending
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chain_of_being" title="Chain of being">
       chain of being
      </a>
      . Through this any lower creation reflects its particular characteristics in Supernal Divinity.
     </p>
     <p>
      Hasidic thought extends the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Divine_immanence" title="Divine immanence">
       Divine immanence
      </a>
      of Kabbalah by holding that God is all that really exists, all else being completely undifferentiated from God's perspective. This view can be defined as monistic panentheism. According to this philosophy, God's existence is higher than anything that this world can express, yet he includes all things of this world within his Divine reality in perfect unity, so that the Creation effected no change in him at all. This paradox is dealt with at length in Chabad texts.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
       <a href="#cite_note-46">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Origin_of_evil">
       Origin of evil
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kabbalah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Origin of evil">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:137px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kaphtziel.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="155" data-file-width="222" height="94" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Kaphtziel.jpg/135px-Kaphtziel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Kaphtziel.jpg/203px-Kaphtziel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fa/Kaphtziel.jpg 2x" width="135"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kaphtziel.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Amulet from the 1400s. Theosophical kabbalists, especially Luria, censored contemporary Practical Kabbalah, but allowed amulets by Sages
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
         <a href="#cite_note-47">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          47
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Among problems considered in the Hebrew Kabbalah is the theological issue of the nature and origin of evil. In the views of some Kabbalists this conceives 'evil' as a 'quality of God', asserting that negativity enters into the essence of the Absolute. In this view it is conceived that the Absolute needs evil to 'be what it is', i.e., to exist.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
       <a href="#cite_note-48">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        48
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Foundational texts of Medieval Kabbalism conceived evil as a demonic parallel to the holy, called the
      <i>
       Sitra Achra
      </i>
      (the "Other Side"), and the
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kelipot" title="Kelipot">
        Kelipot
       </a>
       /Qliphoth
      </i>
      (the "Shells/Husks") that cover and conceal the holy, are nurtured from it, and yet also protect it by limiting its revelation. Scholem termed this element of the Spanish Kabbalah a "Jewish gnostic" motif, in the sense of dual powers in the divine realm of manifestation. In a radical notion, the root of evil is found within the 10 holy Sephirot, through an imbalance of
      <a href="/wiki/Gevurah" title="Gevurah">
       Gevurah
      </a>
      , the power of "Strength/Judgement/Severity".
     </p>
     <p>
      Gevurah is necessary for Creation to exist as it counterposes
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chesed_(Kabbalah)" title="Chesed (Kabbalah)">
       Chesed
      </a>
      ("loving-kindness"), restricting the unlimited divine bounty within suitable vessels, so forming the Worlds. However, if man sins (actualising impure judgement within his soul), the supernal Judgement is reciprocally empowered over the Kindness, introducing disharmony among the Sephirot in the divine realm and exile from God throughout Creation. The demonic realm, though illusory in its holy origin, becomes the real apparent realm of impurity in lower Creation.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Role_of_Man">
       Role of Man
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kabbalah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Role of Man">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%D7%A6%D7%99%D7%95%D7%9F_%D7%A8%D7%91%D7%99_%D7%99%D7%95%D7%A1%D7%A3_%D7%A7%D7%90%D7%A8%D7%95.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3968" data-file-width="2976" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/%D7%A6%D7%99%D7%95%D7%9F_%D7%A8%D7%91%D7%99_%D7%99%D7%95%D7%A1%D7%A3_%D7%A7%D7%90%D7%A8%D7%95.JPG/120px-%D7%A6%D7%99%D7%95%D7%9F_%D7%A8%D7%91%D7%99_%D7%99%D7%95%D7%A1%D7%A3_%D7%A7%D7%90%D7%A8%D7%95.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/%D7%A6%D7%99%D7%95%D7%9F_%D7%A8%D7%91%D7%99_%D7%99%D7%95%D7%A1%D7%A3_%D7%A7%D7%90%D7%A8%D7%95.JPG/180px-%D7%A6%D7%99%D7%95%D7%9F_%D7%A8%D7%91%D7%99_%D7%99%D7%95%D7%A1%D7%A3_%D7%A7%D7%90%D7%A8%D7%95.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/%D7%A6%D7%99%D7%95%D7%9F_%D7%A8%D7%91%D7%99_%D7%99%D7%95%D7%A1%D7%A3_%D7%A7%D7%90%D7%A8%D7%95.JPG/240px-%D7%A6%D7%99%D7%95%D7%9F_%D7%A8%D7%91%D7%99_%D7%99%D7%95%D7%A1%D7%A3_%D7%A7%D7%90%D7%A8%D7%95.JPG 2x" width="120"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%D7%A6%D7%99%D7%95%D7%9F_%D7%A8%D7%91%D7%99_%D7%99%D7%95%D7%A1%D7%A3_%D7%A7%D7%90%D7%A8%D7%95.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joseph_Karo" title="Joseph Karo">
         Joseph Karo
        </a>
        's role as both legalist and mystic underscores Kabbalah's spiritualisation of normative Jewish observance
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Kabbalistic doctrine gives man the central role in Creation, as his soul and body correspond to the supernal divine manifestations. In the Christian Kabbalah this scheme was universalised to describe
      <i>
       harmonia mundi
      </i>
      , the harmony of Creation within man.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
       <a href="#cite_note-49">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        49
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In Judaism, it gave a profound spiritualisation of Jewish practice. While the kabbalistic scheme gave a radically innovative, though conceptually continuous, development of mainstream Midrashic and Talmudic Rabbinic notions, kabbalistic thought underscored and invigorated conservative Jewish observance. The esoteric teachings of kabbalah gave the traditional mitzvot observances the central role in spiritual creation, whether the practitioner was learned in this knowledge or not. Accompanying normative Jewish observance and worship with elite mystical kavanot intentions gave them
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theurgic" title="Theurgic">
       theurgic
      </a>
      power, but sincere observance by common folk, especially in the Hasidic popularisation of kabbalah, could replace esoteric abilities. Many kabbalists were also leading legal figures in Judaism, such as Nachmanides and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joseph_Karo" title="Joseph Karo">
       Joseph Karo
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Medieval kabbalah elaborates particular reasons for each Biblical
      <a href="/wiki/Mitzvah" title="Mitzvah">
       mitzvah
      </a>
      , and their role in harmonising the supernal divine flow, uniting masculine and feminine forces on High. With this, the feminine Divine presence in this world is drawn from exile to the Holy One Above. The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/613_mitzvot" title="613 mitzvot">
       613 mitzvot
      </a>
      are embodied in the organs and soul of man. Lurianic kabbalah incorporates this in the more inclusive scheme of Jewish messianic rectification of exiled divinity. Jewish mysticism, in contrast to Divine transcendence rationalist human-centred reasons for Jewish observance, gave Divine-immanent providential cosmic significance to the daily events in the worldly life of man in general, and the spiritual role of Jewish observance in particular.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Levels_of_the_soul">
       Levels of the soul
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kabbalah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Levels of the soul">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Abraham_abulafia.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="355" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Abraham_abulafia.jpg/120px-Abraham_abulafia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Abraham_abulafia.jpg/180px-Abraham_abulafia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Abraham_abulafia.jpg/240px-Abraham_abulafia.jpg 2x" width="120"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Abraham_abulafia.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Building on Kabbalah's conception of the soul,
        <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Abulafia" title="Abraham Abulafia">
         Abraham Abulafia
        </a>
        's meditations included the "inner illumination of" the human form
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
         <a href="#cite_note-50">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          50
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The Kabbalah posits that the human soul has three elements, the
      <i>
       nefesh
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       ru'ach
      </i>
      , and
      <i>
       neshamah
      </i>
      . The
      <i>
       nefesh
      </i>
      is found in all humans, and enters the physical body at birth. It is the source of one's physical and psychological nature. The next two parts of the soul are not implanted at birth, but can be developed over time; their development depends on the actions and beliefs of the individual. They are said to only fully exist in people awakened spiritually. A common way of explaining the three parts of the soul is as follows:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <i>
        Nefesh
       </i>
       (): the lower part, or "animal part", of the soul. It is linked to instincts and bodily cravings. This part of the soul is provided at birth.
      </li>
      <li>
       <i>
        Ruach
       </i>
       (): the middle soul, the "spirit". It contains the moral virtues and the ability to distinguish between good and evil.
      </li>
      <li>
       <i>
        Neshamah
       </i>
       (): the higher soul, or "super-soul". This separates man from all other life-forms. It is related to the
       <a href="/wiki/Intellect" title="Intellect">
        intellect
       </a>
       and allows man to enjoy and benefit from
       <a href="/wiki/Jewish_eschatology#.22The_World_to_Come.22" title="Jewish eschatology">
        the afterlife
       </a>
       . It allows one to have some awareness of the existence and presence of God.
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      The
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Raaya_Meheimna&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Raaya Meheimna (page does not exist)">
       Raaya Meheimna
      </a>
      , a section of related teachings spread throughout the Zohar, discusses fourth and fifth parts of the human soul, the
      <i>
       chayyah
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       yehidah
      </i>
      (first mentioned in the Midrash Rabbah). Gershom Scholem writes that these "were considered to represent the sublimest levels of intuitive cognition, and to be within the grasp of only a few chosen individuals". The Chayyah and the Yechidah do not enter into the body like the other threethus they received less attention in other sections of the
      <i>
       Zohar
      </i>
      .
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <i>
        Chayyah
       </i>
       (): The part of the soul that allows one to have an awareness of the divine life force itself.
      </li>
      <li>
       <i>
        Yehidah
       </i>
       (): The highest plane of the soul, in which one can achieve as full a union with God as is possible.
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      Both rabbinic and kabbalistic works posit that there are a few additional, non-permanent states of the soul that people can develop on certain occasions. These extra souls, or extra states of the soul, play no part in any afterlife scheme, but are mentioned for completeness:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <i>
        Ruach HaKodesh
       </i>
       ( ) ("spirit of holiness"): a state of the soul that makes prophecy possible. Since the age of classical prophecy passed, no one (outside of Israel) receives the soul of prophecy any longer.
      </li>
      <li>
       <i>
        Neshamah Yeseira
       </i>
       : The "supplemental soul" that a Jew can experience on
       <a href="/wiki/Shabbat" title="Shabbat">
        Shabbat
       </a>
       . It makes possible an enhanced spiritual enjoyment of the day. This exists only when one is observing Shabbat; it can be lost and gained depending on one's observance.
      </li>
      <li>
       <i>
        Neshamah Kedosha
       </i>
       : Provided to Jews at the age of maturity (13 for boys, 12 for girls) and is related to the study and fulfillment of the Torah commandments. It exists only when one studies and follows the Torah; it can be lost and gained depending on one's study and observance.
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Reincarnation">
       Reincarnation
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kabbalah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Reincarnation">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Gilgul" title="Gilgul">
       Gilgul
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Reincarnation" title="Reincarnation">
       Reincarnation
      </a>
      , the transmigration of the soul after death, was introduced into Judaism as a central esoteric tenet of Kabbalah from the Medieval period onwards, called
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gilgul_neshamot" title="Gilgul neshamot">
       Gilgul neshamot
      </a>
      ("Cycles of the soul"). The concept does not appear overtly in the Hebrew Bible or classic Rabbinic literature, and was rejected by various Medieval Jewish philosophers. However, the Kabbalists explained a number of scriptural passages in reference to Gilgulim. The concept became central to the later Kabbalah of Isaac Luria, who systemised it as the personal parallel to the cosmic process of rectification. Through Lurianic Kabbalah and Hasidic Judaism, reincarnation entered popular Jewish culture as a literary motif.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Tzimtzum.2C_Shevirah_and_Tikkun">
       Tzimtzum, Shevirah and Tikkun
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kabbalah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Tzimtzum, Shevirah and Tikkun">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:142px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PikiWiki_Israel_11324_The_city_wore_white.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1191" data-file-width="1782" height="94" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/PikiWiki_Israel_11324_The_city_wore_white.jpg/140px-PikiWiki_Israel_11324_The_city_wore_white.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/PikiWiki_Israel_11324_The_city_wore_white.jpg/210px-PikiWiki_Israel_11324_The_city_wore_white.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/PikiWiki_Israel_11324_The_city_wore_white.jpg/280px-PikiWiki_Israel_11324_The_city_wore_white.jpg 2x" width="140"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PikiWiki_Israel_11324_The_city_wore_white.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        16th century graves of Safed, Galilee. The messianic focus of its mystical renaissance culminated in Lurianic thought
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      After publication of the Zohar in the late 13th century, attempts were made to interpret and systemise the doctrines within its imagery. This culminated in the successive, comprehensive expositions of Cordovero and Luria in 16th century Safed. While Cordovero systemised Medieval kabbalah in a rationally influenced linear scheme, this was subsequently superseded by the mythological, dynamic scheme of Isaac Luria, recorded by Chaim Vital and his other disciples. Lurianic theosophy became the foundation of modern kabbalah, incorporating Medieval theosophy within its wider explanation. The supra-rational Lurianic doctrines of Tzimtzum, Shevirah and Tikkun reorganised Kabbalistic doctrine around crisis-catharsis Divine exile and redemption, explaining
      <a href="/wiki/Jewish_messianism" title="Jewish messianism">
       Jewish messianism
      </a>
      in Kabbalah.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <i>
       Tzimtzum
      </i>
      (Constriction/Concentration) is the primordial cosmic act whereby God "contracted" His infinite light, leaving a "void" into which the light of existence was poured. This allowed the emergence of independent existence that would not become nullified by the pristine Infinite Light, reconciling the unity of the Ein Sof with the plurality of creation. This changed the first creative act into one of withdrawal/exile, the antithesis of the ultimate Divine Will. In contrast, a new emanation after the Tzimtzum shone into the vacuum to begin creation, but led to an initial instability called
      <i>
       Tohu
      </i>
      (Chaos), leading to a new crisis of
      <i>
       Shevirah
      </i>
      (Shattering) of the sephirot vessels. The shards of the broken vessels fell down into the lower realms, animated by remnants of their divine light, causing primordial exile within the Divine Persona before the creation of man. Exile and enclothement of higher divinity within lower realms throughout existence requires man to complete the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Tikkun_olam" title="Tikkun olam">
        Tikkun olam
       </a>
      </i>
      (Rectification) process. Rectification Above corresponds to the reorganization of the independent sefirot into relating
      <i>
       Partzufim
      </i>
      (Divine Personas), previously referred to obliquely in the Zohar. From the catastrophe stems the possibility of self-aware Creation, and also the
      <i>
       Kelipot
      </i>
      (Impure Shells) of previous Medieval kabbalah. The metaphorical
      <a href="/wiki/Anthropomorphism_in_Kabbalah" title="Anthropomorphism in Kabbalah">
       anthropomorphism
      </a>
      of the partzufim accentuates the sexual unifications of the redemption process, while
      <a href="/wiki/Gilgul" title="Gilgul">
       Gilgul
      </a>
      reincarnation emerges from the scheme. Uniquely, Lurianism gave formerly private mysticism the urgency of Messianic social involvement.
     </p>
     <p>
      According to interpretations of Luria, the catastrophe stemmed from the "unwillingness" of the residue imprint after the Tzimtzum to relate to the new vitality that began creation. The process was arranged to shed and harmonise the Divine Infinity with the latent potential of evil.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
       <a href="#cite_note-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        51
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The creation of
      <a href="/wiki/Adam" title="Adam">
       Adam
      </a>
      would have redeemed existence, but his sin caused new shevirah of Divine vitality, requiring the Giving of the Torah to begin Messianic rectification. Historical and individual history becomes the narrative of reclaiming exiled Divine sparks.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Linguistic_mysticism_of_Hebrew">
       Linguistic mysticism of Hebrew
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kabbalah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Linguistic mysticism of Hebrew">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:132px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rakia-letters.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="98" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Rakia-letters.jpg/130px-Rakia-letters.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Rakia-letters.jpg/195px-Rakia-letters.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Rakia-letters.jpg/260px-Rakia-letters.jpg 2x" width="130"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rakia-letters.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Kabbalistic painting of the supernal illumination of Hebrew letters in Creation
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Kabbalistic thought extended Biblical and Midrashic notions that God enacted Creation through the Hebrew language and through the Torah into a full linguistic mysticism. In this, every Hebrew letter, word, number, even accent on words of the Hebrew Bible contain esoteric meanings, describing the spiritual dimensions within exoteric ideas, and it teaches the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hermeneutic" title="Hermeneutic">
       hermeneutic
      </a>
      methods of interpretation for ascertaining these meanings. Names of God in Judaism have further prominence, though fluidity of meaning turns the whole Torah into a Divine name. As the Hebrew name of things is the channel of their lifeforce, parallel to the sephirot, so concepts such as "holiness" and "
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mitzvot" title="Mitzvot">
       mitzvot
      </a>
      " embody ontological Divine immanence, as God can be known in manifestation as well as transcendence. The infinite potential of meaning in the Torah, as in the Ein Sof, is reflected in the symbol of the two trees of the Garden of Eden; the Torah of the
      <a href="/wiki/Tree_of_the_knowledge_of_good_and_evil" title="Tree of the knowledge of good and evil">
       Tree of Knowledge
      </a>
      is the external, Halachic Torah, through which mystics can perceive the unlimited Torah of the Tree of Life. In Lurianic expression, each of the 600,000 souls of Israel find their own interpretation in Torah.
     </p>
     <blockquote class="templatequote">
      <p>
       "The reapers of the Field are the Comrades, masters of this wisdom, because
       <i>
        Malkhut
       </i>
       is called the Apple Field, and She grows sprouts of secrets and new meanings of Torah. Those who constantly create new interpretations of Torah are the ones who reap Her."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
        <a href="#cite_note-52">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         52
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      As early as the 1st century BCE Jews believed that the Torah (first five books of the Hebrew Bible) and wider canonical texts contained encoded messages and hidden meanings.
      <a href="/wiki/Gematria" title="Gematria">
       Gematria
      </a>
      is one method for discovering its hidden meanings. Each letter in Hebrew also represents a number; Hebrew, unlike many other languages, never developed a separate numerical alphabet. By converting letters to numbers, Kabbalists were able to find a hidden meaning in each word. This method of interpretation was used extensively by various schools.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Primary_texts">
       Primary texts
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kabbalah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Primary texts">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:112px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zohar.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="718" data-file-width="450" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Zohar.png/110px-Zohar.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Zohar.png/165px-Zohar.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Zohar.png/220px-Zohar.png 2x" width="110"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zohar.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Title page of first printed edition of the
        <a href="/wiki/Zohar" title="Zohar">
         Zohar
        </a>
        , main sourcebook of Kabbalah, from
        <a href="/wiki/Mantua" title="Mantua">
         Mantua
        </a>
        , Italy in 1558
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Kabbalah:_Primary_texts" title="Kabbalah: Primary texts">
       Kabbalah: Primary texts
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Like the rest of the Rabbinic literature, the texts of kabbalah were once part of an ongoing oral tradition, though, over the centuries, much of the oral tradition has been written down.
     </p>
     <p>
      Jewish forms of esotericism existed over 2,000 years ago.
      <a href="/wiki/Ben_Sira" title="Ben Sira">
       Ben Sira
      </a>
      (born c. 170 BCE) warns against it, saying: "You shall have no business with secret things".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
       <a href="#cite_note-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Nonetheless, mystical studies were undertaken and resulted in mystical literature, the first being the
      <a href="/wiki/Apocalyptic_literature" title="Apocalyptic literature">
       Apocalyptic literature
      </a>
      of the second and first pre-Christian centuries and which contained elements that carried over to later kabbalah.
     </p>
     <p>
      Throughout the centuries since, many texts have been produced, among them the ancient descriptions of
      <i>
       Sefer Yetzirah
      </i>
      , the
      <i>
       Heichalot
      </i>
      mystical ascent literature, the
      <i>
       Bahir
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Sefer_Raziel_HaMalakh" title="Sefer Raziel HaMalakh">
        Sefer Raziel HaMalakh
       </a>
      </i>
      and the
      <i>
       Zohar
      </i>
      , the main text of Kabbalistic exegesis. Classic mystical Bible commentaries are included in fuller versions of the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Mikraot_Gedolot" title="Mikraot Gedolot">
        Mikraot Gedolot
       </a>
      </i>
      (Main Commentators). Cordoveran systemisation is presented in
      <i>
       Pardes Rimonim
      </i>
      , philosophical articulation in the works of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maharal" title="Maharal">
       Maharal
      </a>
      , and Lurianic rectification in
      <i>
       Etz Chayim
      </i>
      . Subsequent interpretation of Lurianic Kabbalah was made in the writings of Shalom Sharabi, in
      <i>
       Nefesh HaChaim
      </i>
      and the 20th-century
      <i>
       Sulam
      </i>
      . Hasidism interpreted kabbalistic structures to their correspondence in inward perception.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
       <a href="#cite_note-54">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Hasidic development of kabbalah incorporates a successive stage of Jewish mysticism from historical kabbalistic metaphysics.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
       <a href="#cite_note-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Scholarship">
       Scholarship
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kabbalah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Scholarship">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_Jewish_mysticism_scholars" title="List of Jewish mysticism scholars">
       List of Jewish mysticism scholars
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      The first modern-academic historians of Judaism, the "
      <a href="/wiki/Wissenschaft_des_Judentums" title="Wissenschaft des Judentums">
       Wissenschaft des Judentums
      </a>
      " school of the 19th century, framed Judaism in solely rational terms in the emancipatory Haskalah spirit of their age. They opposed kabbalah and restricted its significance from Jewish historiography. In the mid-20th century, it was left to Gershom Scholem to overturn their stance, establishing the flourishing present-day academic investigation of Jewish mysticism, and making Heichalot, Kabbalistic and Hasidic texts the objects of scholarly critical-historical study. In Scholem's opinion, the mythical and mystical components of Judaism were at least as important as the rational ones, and he thought that they, rather than the exoteric Halakha, were the living current in historical Jewish development.
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_University_of_Jerusalem" title="Hebrew University of Jerusalem">
       Hebrew University of Jerusalem
      </a>
      has been a centre of this research, including Scholem and
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Isaiah_Tishby&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Isaiah Tishby (page does not exist)">
       Isaiah Tishby
      </a>
      , and more recently
      <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Dan" title="Joseph Dan">
       Joseph Dan
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Yehuda_Liebes&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Yehuda Liebes (page does not exist)">
       Yehuda Liebes
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Rachel_Elior" title="Rachel Elior">
       Rachel Elior
      </a>
      , and Moshe Idel.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
       <a href="#cite_note-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Scholars across the eras of Jewish mysticism in America and Britain have included
      <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Green" title="Arthur Green">
       Arthur Green
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Lawrence_Fine&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Lawrence Fine (page does not exist)">
       Lawrence Fine
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elliot_Wolfson" title="Elliot Wolfson">
       Elliot Wolfson
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Daniel_C._Matt" title="Daniel C. Matt">
       Daniel Matt
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
       <a href="#cite_note-57">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        57
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ada_Rapoport-Albert&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ada Rapoport-Albert (page does not exist)">
       Ada Rapoport-Albert
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Scholars in the present generation have revised early theories including Scholem's, on such questions as Heichalot mysticism and a Jewish "gnosticism", the origins of Kabbalah, and the sources of Hasidism.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2012)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      Moshe Idel has opened up research on the Ecstatic Kabbalah alongside the theosophical, and has called for new multi-disciplinary approaches, beyond the philological and historical that have dominated until now, to include
      <a href="/wiki/Religious_studies#Phenomenology" title="Religious studies">
       phenomenology
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Religious_studies#Psychology_of_religion" title="Religious studies">
       psychology
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Religious_studies#Anthropology_of_religion" title="Religious studies">
       anthropology
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comparative_studies" title="Comparative studies">
       comparative studies
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
       <a href="#cite_note-58">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        58
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Claims_for_authority">
       Claims for authority
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kabbalah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Claims for authority">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Historians have noted that most claims for the authority of kabbalah involve an argument of the antiquity of authority (see, e.g., Joseph Dan's discussion in his
      <i>
       Circle of the Unique Cherub
      </i>
      ). As a result, virtually all early foundational works
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pseudepigraph" title="Pseudepigraph">
       pseudepigraphically
      </a>
      claim, or are ascribed, ancient authorship. For example,
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sefer_Raziel_HaMalach" title="Sefer Raziel HaMalach">
        Sefer Raziel HaMalach
       </a>
      </i>
      , an astro-magical text partly based on a magical manual of late antiquity,
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sefer_ha-Razim" title="Sefer ha-Razim">
        Sefer ha-Razim
       </a>
      </i>
      , was, according to the kabbalists, transmitted by the angel
      <a href="/wiki/Raziel" title="Raziel">
       Raziel
      </a>
      to Adam after he was evicted from Eden.
     </p>
     <p>
      Another famous work, the early
      <i>
       Sefer Yetzirah
      </i>
      , supposedly dates back to the patriarch
      <a href="/wiki/Abraham" title="Abraham">
       Abraham
      </a>
      . This tendency toward pseudepigraphy has its roots in apocalyptic literature, which claims that esoteric knowledge such as magic, divination and astrology was transmitted to humans in the mythic past by the two angels, Aza and
      <a href="/wiki/Azazel" title="Azazel">
       Azaz'el
      </a>
      (in other places, Azaz'el and Uzaz'el) who fell from heaven (see Genesis 6:4).
     </p>
