     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Dualistic_cosmology">
       Dualistic cosmology
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kabbalah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Dualistic cosmology">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Although Kabbalah propounds the Unity of God, one of the most serious and sustained criticisms is that it may lead away from monotheism, and instead promote
      <a href="/wiki/Dualism" title="Dualism">
       dualism
      </a>
      , the belief that there is a supernatural counterpart to God. The dualistic system holds that there is a good power versus an evil power. There are two primary models of Gnostic-dualistic cosmology: the first, which goes back to
      <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">
       Zoroastrianism
      </a>
      , believes creation is ontologically divided between good and evil forces; the second, found largely in Greco-Roman
      <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">
       metaphysics
      </a>
      like
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neo-Platonism" title="Neo-Platonism">
       Neo-Platonism
      </a>
      , argues that the universe knew a primordial harmony, but that a cosmic disruption yielded a second, evil, dimension to reality. This second model influenced the cosmology of the Kabbalah.
     </p>
     <p>
      According to Kabbalistic cosmology, the Ten Sefirot correspond to ten levels of creation. These levels of creation must not be understood as ten different "gods" but as ten different ways of revealing God, one per level. It is not God who changes but the ability to perceive God that changes.
     </p>
     <p>
      While God may seem to exhibit dual natures (masculine-feminine, compassionate-judgmental, creator-creation), all adherents of Kabbalah have consistently stressed the ultimate unity of God. For example, in all discussions of Male and Female, the hidden nature of God exists above it all without limit, being called the Infinite or the "No End" (Ein Sof)neither one nor the other, transcending any definition. The ability of God to become hidden from perception is called "Restriction" (Tzimtzum). Hiddenness makes creation possible because God can become "revealed" in a diversity of limited ways, which then form the building blocks of creation.
     </p>
     <p>
      Kabbalistic texts, including the
      <i>
       Zohar
      </i>
      , appear to affirm dualism, as they ascribe all evil to the separation from holiness known as the Sitra Achra
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
       <a href="#cite_note-59">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      ("the other side") which is opposed to
      <i>
       Sitra D'Kedushah
      </i>
      , or the Side of Holiness.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
       <a href="#cite_note-60">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        60
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The "left side" of divine emanation is a negative mirror image of the "side of holiness" with which it was locked in combat. [
      <i>
       Encyclopaedia Judaica
      </i>
      , Volume 6, "Dualism", p.244]. While this evil aspect exists within the divine structure of the Sefirot, the
      <i>
       Zohar
      </i>
      indicates that the Sitra Ahra has no power over Ein Sof, and only exists as a necessary aspect of the creation of God to give man free choice, and that evil is the consequence of this choice. It is not a supernatural force opposed to God, but a reflection of the inner moral combat within mankind between the dictates of morality and the surrender to one's basic instincts.
     </p>
     <p>
      Rabbi Dr. David Gottlieb notes that many Kabbalists hold that the concepts of, e.g., a Heavenly Court or the Sitra Ahra are only given to humanity by God as a working model to understand His ways within our own epistemological limits. They reject the notion that a
      <a href="/wiki/Satan" title="Satan">
       satan
      </a>
      or
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angels" title="Angels">
       angels
      </a>
      actually exist. Others hold that non-divine spiritual entities were indeed created by God as a means for exacting his will.
     </p>
     <p>
      According to Kabbalists, humans cannot yet understand the infinity of God. Rather, there is God as revealed to humans (corresponding to
      <a href="/wiki/Zeir_Anpin" title="Zeir Anpin">
       Zeir Anpin
      </a>
      ), and the rest of the infinity of God as remaining hidden from human experience (corresponding to Arich Anpin).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
       <a href="#cite_note-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        61
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      One reading of this theology is monotheistic, similar to
      <a href="/wiki/Panentheism" title="Panentheism">
       panentheism
      </a>
      ; another reading of the same theology is that it is dualistic. Gershom Scholem writes:
     </p>
     <blockquote class="toccolours" style="float:none; display:table;">
      <div style="padding: 10px 15px 10px 15px;">
       It is clear that with this postulate of an impersonal basic reality in God, which becomes a personor appears as a persononly in the process of Creation and Revelation, Kabbalism abandons the personalistic basis of the Biblical conception of God....It will not surprise us to find that speculation has run the whole gamutfrom attempts to re-transform the impersonal
       <i>
        En-Sof
       </i>
       into the personal God of the Bible to the downright heretical doctrine of a genuine dualism between the hidden Ein Sof and the personal Demiurge of Scripture.
       <br/>
       
       <i>
        Major Trends in Jewish Mysticism
       </i>
       Shocken Books (p.1112)
      </div>
     </blockquote>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Distinction_between_Jews_and_non-Jews">
       Distinction between Jews and non-Jews
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kabbalah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Distinction between Jews and non-Jews">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      A number of medieval Kabbalistic sources contain statements to the effect that the Jewish soul is ontologically different from the soul of non-Jews; for example, it is held by some that Jews have three levels of soul,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Nefesh" title="Nefesh">
        nefesh
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ruach" title="Ruach">
        ruach
       </a>
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neshamah" title="Neshamah">
        neshamah
       </a>
      </i>
      while non-Jews have only
      <i>
       nefesh
      </i>
      . The Zohar comments on the Biblical verse which states "Let the waters teem with swarms of creatures that have a living soul" as follows: "The verse 'creatures that have a living soul,' pertains to the Jews, for they are the children of God, and from God come their holy souls....And the souls of the other nations, from where do they come? Rabbi Elazar says that they have souls from the impure left side, and therefore they are all impure, defiling anyone who comes near them" (Zohar commentary on Genesis).
     </p>
     <p>
      Such theologically framed hostility may have been a response to some medieval demonization of Jews which developed in some parts of Western and Christian society and thought, starting with the Patristic writings.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
       <a href="#cite_note-62">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        62
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      According to Isaac Luria (153472) and other commentators on the Zohar, righteous Gentiles do not have this demonic aspect and are in many ways similar to Jewish souls. A number of prominent Kabbalists, e.g., Rabbi Pinchas Eliyahu of Vilna, the author of
      <i>
       Sefer ha-Brit
      </i>
      , held that only some marginal elements in the humanity represent these demonic forces. On the other hand, the souls of Jewish heretics have much more satanic energy than the worst of idol worshippers; this view is popular in some Hasidic circles, especially
      <a href="/wiki/Satmar_(Hasidic_dynasty)" title="Satmar (Hasidic dynasty)">
       Satmar Hasidim
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Some later Kabbalistic works build and elaborate on these ideas. One point of view is represented by the Hasidic work
      <a href="/wiki/Tanya" title="Tanya">
       Tanya
      </a>
      (1797), in order to argue that Jews have a different character of soul: while a non-Jew, according to the author Rabbi
      <a href="/wiki/Shneur_Zalman_of_Liadi" title="Shneur Zalman of Liadi">
       Shneur Zalman of Liadi
      </a>
      (b. 1745), can achieve a high level of spirituality, similar to an angel, his soul is still fundamentally different in character, but not value, from a Jewish one.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
       <a href="#cite_note-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A similar view is found in early medieval philosophical book
      <a href="/wiki/Kuzari" title="Kuzari">
       Kuzari
      </a>
      , by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yehuda_Halevi" title="Yehuda Halevi">
       Yehuda Halevi
      </a>
      (1075-1141 AD).
     </p>
     <p>
      On the other hand, many prominent Kabbalists rejected this idea and believed in essential equality of all human souls.
      <a href="/wiki/Menahem_Azariah_da_Fano" title="Menahem Azariah da Fano">
       Menahem Azariah da Fano
      </a>
      (15481620), in his book
      <i>
       Reincarnations of souls
      </i>
      , provides many examples of non-Jewish Biblical figures being reincarnated into Jews and vice versa; the contemporary
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Habad" title="Habad">
       Habad
      </a>
      Rabbi and mystic
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dov_Ber_Pinson" title="Dov Ber Pinson">
       Dov Ber Pinson
      </a>
      teaches that distinctions between Jews and non-Jews in works such as the Tanya are not to be understood as literally referring to the external properties of a person (what religious community they are born into), but rather as referring to the properties of souls as they can be re-incarnated in any religious community.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_64-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-64">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        64
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Another prominent Habad Rabbi,
      <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Yehudah_Khein" title="Abraham Yehudah Khein">
       Abraham Yehudah Khein
      </a>
      (b. 1878), believed that spiritually elevated Gentiles have essentially Jewish souls, "who just lack the formal conversion to Judaism", and that unspiritual Jews are "Jewish merely by their birth documents".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
       <a href="#cite_note-65">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        65
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The great 20th-century Kabbalist Yehuda Ashlag viewed the terms "Jews" and "Gentile" as different levels of perception, available to every human soul.
     </p>
     <p>
      David Halperin
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
       <a href="#cite_note-66">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        66
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      argues that the collapse of Kabbalah's influence among Western European Jews over the course of the 17th and 18th century was a result of the cognitive dissonance they experienced between the negative perception of Gentiles found in some exponents of Kabbalah, and their own positive dealings with non-Jews, which were rapidly expanding and improving during this period due to the influence of the Enlightenment.
     </p>
     <p>
      However, a number of renowned Kabbalists claimed the exact opposite. In their view, Kabbalah transcends the borders of Judaism and can serve as a basis of inter-religious theosophy and a universal religion. Rabbi
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Pinchas_Elijah_Hurwitz&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Pinchas Elijah Hurwitz (page does not exist)">
       Pinchas Elijah Hurwitz
      </a>
      , a prominent Lithuanian-Galician Kabbalist of the 18th century and a moderate proponent of the Haskalah, called for brotherly love and solidarity between all nations, and believed that Kabbalah can empower everyone, Jews and Gentiles alike, with prophetic abilities.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
       <a href="#cite_note-67">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The works of
      <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Cohen_de_Herrera" title="Abraham Cohen de Herrera">
       Abraham Cohen de Herrera
      </a>
      (15701635) are full of references to Gentile mystical philosophers. Such approach was particularly common among the Renaissance and post-Renaissance
      <a href="/wiki/Italian_Jews" title="Italian Jews">
       Italian Jews
      </a>
      . Late medieval and Renaissance Italian Kabbalists, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Yohanan_Alemanno" title="Yohanan Alemanno">
       Yohanan Alemanno
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/David_Messer_Leon" title="David Messer Leon">
       David Messer Leon
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Yagel" title="Abraham Yagel">
       Abraham Yagel
      </a>
      , adhered to humanistic ideals and incorporated teachings of various Christian and pagan mystics.
     </p>
     <p>
      A prime representative of this humanist stream in Kabbalah was Rabbi
      <a href="/wiki/Elijah_Benamozegh" title="Elijah Benamozegh">
       Elijah Benamozegh
      </a>
      , who explicitly praised Christianity, Islam, Zoroastrianism, Hinduism, as well as a whole range of ancient pagan mystical systems. He believed that Kabbalah can reconcile the differences between the world religions, which represent different facets and stages of the universal human spirituality. In his writings, Benamozegh interprets the
      <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">
       New Testament
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">
       Hadith
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Vedas" title="Vedas">
       Vedas
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Avesta" title="Avesta">
       Avesta
      </a>
      and pagan mysteries according to the Kabbalistic theosophy.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
       <a href="#cite_note-68">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        68
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      For a different perspective, see Wolfson.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
       <a href="#cite_note-69">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        69
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He provides numerous examples from the 17th to the 20th centuries, which would challenge the view of Halperin cited above as well as the notion that "modern Judaism" has rejected or dismissed this "outdated aspect" of the religion and, he argues, there are still Kabbalists today who harbor this view. He argues that, while it is accurate to say that many Jews do and would find this distinction offensive, it is inaccurate to say that the idea has been totally rejected in all circles. As Wolfson has argued, it is an ethical demand on the part of scholars to continue to be vigilant with regard to this matter and in this way the tradition can be refined from within.
     </p>
     <p>
      However, as explained above, many well known Kabbalists rejected the literal interpretation of these seemingly discriminatory views. They argued that the term "Jew" was to be interpreted metaphorically, as referring to the spiritual development of the soul, rather than the superficial denomination of the individual, and they added a chain of intermediary states between "Jews" and idol worshippers, or spiritualised the very definition of "Jews" and "non-Jews" and argued that a soul can be re-incarnated in different communities (whether Jewish or not) as much as within a single one.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_64-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-64">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        64
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval_views">
       Medieval views
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kabbalah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Medieval views">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:132px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Knesset_Menorah_Spain.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="488" data-file-width="432" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Knesset_Menorah_Spain.jpg/130px-Knesset_Menorah_Spain.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Knesset_Menorah_Spain.jpg/195px-Knesset_Menorah_Spain.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Knesset_Menorah_Spain.jpg/260px-Knesset_Menorah_Spain.jpg 2x" width="130"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Knesset_Menorah_Spain.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Golden_age_of_Jewish_culture_in_Spain" title="Golden age of Jewish culture in Spain">
         Golden age of Spanish Judaism
        </a>
        on the
        <a href="/wiki/Knesset_Menorah" title="Knesset Menorah">
         Knesset Menorah
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Maimonides" title="Maimonides">
         Maimonides
        </a>
        holding
        <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">
         Aristotle
        </a>
        's work
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:132px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Knesset_Menorah_P5200009J.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="367" data-file-width="413" height="116" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Knesset_Menorah_P5200009J.JPG/130px-Knesset_Menorah_P5200009J.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Knesset_Menorah_P5200009J.JPG/195px-Knesset_Menorah_P5200009J.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Knesset_Menorah_P5200009J.JPG/260px-Knesset_Menorah_P5200009J.JPG 2x" width="130"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Knesset_Menorah_P5200009J.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Kabbalah mysticism on the
        <a href="/wiki/Knesset_Menorah" title="Knesset Menorah">
         Knesset Menorah
        </a>
        , which shared some similarities of theory with Jewish Neoplatonists
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The idea that there are ten divine
      <i>
       sefirot
      </i>
      could evolve over time into the idea that "God is One being, yet in that One being there are Ten" which opens up a debate about what the "correct beliefs" in God should be, according to Judaism. Rabbi
      <a href="/wiki/Saadia_Gaon" title="Saadia Gaon">
       Saadia Gaon
      </a>
      teaches in his book
      <a href="/wiki/Emunoth_ve-Deoth" title="Emunoth ve-Deoth">
       Emunot v'Deot
      </a>
      that Jews who believe in reincarnation have adopted a non-Jewish belief.
     </p>
     <p>
      Maimonides (12th century) rejected many of the texts of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hekalot" title="Hekalot">
       Hekalot
      </a>
      , particularly
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Shi%27ur_Qomah" title="Shi'ur Qomah">
        Shi'ur Qomah
       </a>
      </i>
      whose starkly anthropomorphic vision of God he considered heretical.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
       <a href="#cite_note-70">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        70
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Nachmanides (13th century) provides background to many kabbalistic ideas. His works offer in-depth of various concepts. In fact, an entire book, entitled
      <a class="external text" href="http://www.hebrewbooks.org/38461" rel="nofollow">
       <i>
        Gevuras Aryeh
       </i>
      </a>
      , was authored by Rabbi
      <a href="/wiki/Yaakov_Yehuda_Aryeh_Leib_Frenkel" title="Yaakov Yehuda Aryeh Leib Frenkel">
       Yaakov Yehuda Aryeh Leib Frenkel
      </a>
      and originally published in 1915, specifically to explain and elaborate on the kabbalistic concepts addressed by Nachmanides in his commentary to the Five books of Moses
     </p>
     <p>
      Rabbi
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Avraham_son_of_Rambam" title="Avraham son of Rambam">
       Abraham ben Moses ben Maimon
      </a>
      , in the spirit of his father Maimonides, Rabbi
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saadiah_Gaon" title="Saadiah Gaon">
       Saadiah Gaon
      </a>
      , and other predecessors, explains at length in his
      <i>
       Milamot HaShem
      </i>
      that God is in no way literally within time or space nor physically outside time or space, since time and space simply do not apply to his being whatsoever. This is in contrast to certain popular understandings of modern Kabbalah which teach a form of panentheism, that his 'essence' is within everything.
     </p>
     <p>
      Around the 1230s,
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Rabbi_Meir_ben_Simon_of_Narbonne&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Rabbi Meir ben Simon of Narbonne (page does not exist)">
       Rabbi Meir ben Simon of Narbonne
      </a>
      wrote an epistle (included in his
      <i>
       Milemet Mitzvah
      </i>
      ) against his contemporaries, the early Kabbalists, characterizing them as blasphemers who even approach heresy. He particularly singled out the Sefer Bahir, rejecting the attribution of its authorship to the
      <i>
       tanna
      </i>
      <a href="/wiki/Nehunya_ben_HaKanah" title="Nehunya ben HaKanah">
       R. Neunya ben ha-Kanah
      </a>
      and describing some of its content as truly heretical.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Isaac_ben_Sheshet" title="Isaac ben Sheshet">
       Rabbi Yitzchak ben Sheshet Perfet
      </a>
      (The
      <i>
       Rivash
      </i>
      ), 13261408. Although as is evident from his response on the topic (157) the Rivash was skeptical of certain interpretations of Kabbalah popular in his time, it is equally evident that overall he did accept Kabbalah as received Jewish wisdom, and attempted to defend it from attackers. To this end he cited and rejected a certain philosopher who claimed that Kabbalah was "worse than Christianity", as it made God into 10, not just into three. Most followers of Kabbalah have never followed this interpretation of Kabbalah, on the grounds that the concept of the Christian Trinity posits that there are three persons existing within the Godhead, one of whom took human form.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2007)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      In contrast, the mainstream understanding of the kabbalistic
      <i>
       sefirot
      </i>
      holds that they have no mind or intelligence; further, they are not addressed in prayer and they cannot become a human being. They are conduits for interaction, not persons or beings. Nonetheless, many important
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Poskim" title="Poskim">
        poskim
       </a>
      </i>
      , such as Maimonides in his
      <a href="/wiki/Mishneh_Torah" title="Mishneh Torah">
       Mishneh Torah
      </a>
      , prohibit any use of mediators between oneself and the creator as a form of idolatry.
     </p>
     <p>
      Rabbi
      <a href="/wiki/Leon_of_Modena" title="Leon of Modena">
       Leone di Modena
      </a>
      , a 17th-century
      <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">
       Venetian
      </a>
      critic of Kabbalah, wrote that if we were to accept the Kabbalah, then the Christian trinity would indeed be compatible with Judaism, as the Trinity closely resembles the kabbalistic doctrine of the
      <i>
       sefirot
      </i>
      . This critique was in response to the knowledge that some European Jews of the period addressed individual
      <i>
       sefirot
      </i>
      in some of their prayers, although the practice was apparently uncommon. Apologists explain that Jews may have been praying
      <i>
       for
      </i>
      and not necessarily
      <i>
       to
      </i>
      the aspects of Godliness represented by the
      <i>
       sefirot
      </i>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yaakov_Emden" title="Yaakov Emden">
       Yaakov Emden
      </a>
      , 16971776, wrote the
      <i>
       Mitpaath Sfarim
      </i>
      (
      <i>
       Veil of the Books
      </i>
      ), a detailed critique of the Zohar in which he concludes that certain parts of the Zohar contain heretical teaching and therefore could not have been written by Shimon bar Yochai.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Orthodox_Judaism">
       Orthodox Judaism
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kabbalah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Orthodox Judaism">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:112px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tikun_l_s.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1023" data-file-width="655" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Tikun_l_s.jpg/110px-Tikun_l_s.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Tikun_l_s.jpg/165px-Tikun_l_s.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Tikun_l_s.jpg/220px-Tikun_l_s.jpg 2x" width="110"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tikun_l_s.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Tikkun for reading through the night of
        <a href="/wiki/Shavuot" title="Shavuot">
         Shavuot
        </a>
        , a popular Jewish custom from the Safed Kabbalists
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Rabbi
      <a href="/wiki/Yi%E1%B8%A5yah_Qafi%E1%B8%A5" title="Yiyah Qafi">
       Yiyeh Qafe
      </a>
      , a 20th-century
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yemenite_Jewish" title="Yemenite Jewish">
       Yemenite Jewish
      </a>
      leader and Chief Rabbi of Yemen, spearheaded the
      <a href="/wiki/Dor_Daim" title="Dor Daim">
       Dor Deah
      </a>
      ("generation of knowledge") movement
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
       <a href="#cite_note-71">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        71
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      to counteract the influence of the
      <a href="/wiki/Zohar" title="Zohar">
       Zohar
      </a>
      and modern Kabbalah.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
       <a href="#cite_note-72">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        72
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He authored critiques of mysticism in general and
      <i>
       Lurianic Kabbalah
      </i>
      in particular; his magnum opus was Milamoth ha-Shem (
      <i>
       Wars of Hashem
      </i>
      )
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
       <a href="#cite_note-73">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        73
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      against what he perceived as neo-platonic and gnostic influences on Judaism with the publication and distribution of the Zohar since the 13th Century. Rabbi Yiyah founded
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yeshivot" title="Yeshivot">
       yeshivot
      </a>
      , rabbinical schools, and synagogues that featured a rationalist approach to Judaism based on the Talmud and works of Saadia Gaon and Maimonides (Rambam).
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Yeshayahu_Leibowitz" title="Yeshayahu Leibowitz">
       Yeshayahu Leibowitz
      </a>
      (19031994), an ultra-rationalist Modern Orthodox philosopher and brother of
      <a href="/wiki/Nechama_Leibowitz" title="Nechama Leibowitz">
       Nechama Leibowitz
      </a>
      , publicly shared views expressed in Rabbi Yiyeh Qafe's book '
      <b>
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.yahadut.org.il/zohar/milhamot-hashem.pdf" rel="nofollow">
        Milamoth HaShem
       </a>
       '
      </b>
      against mysticism. For example, Leibowitz called Kabbalah "a collection of "pagan superstitions" and "idol worship" in remarks given after receiving the
      <a href="/wiki/Yakir_Yerushalayim" title="Yakir Yerushalayim">
       Yakir Yerushalayim
      </a>
      Award (English: worthy citizen of Jerusalem) in 1990.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
       <a href="#cite_note-74">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        74
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In modern times, rationalists holding similar views aligned with the rationalism of Dor Daim have described themselves as "talmide ha-Rambam" (disciples of Maimonides) rather than Dor Daim, and are more theologically aligned with the rationalism of
      <a href="/wiki/Modern_Orthodox_Judaism" title="Modern Orthodox Judaism">
       Modern Orthodox Judaism
      </a>
      than with Orthodox asidic or
      <a href="/wiki/Haredi_Judaism" title="Haredi Judaism">
       aredi
      </a>
      communities.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
       <a href="#cite_note-75">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        75
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      There is dispute among modern Haredim as to the status of Isaac Luria's, the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arizal" title="Arizal">
       Arizal
      </a>
      's Kabbalistic teachings. While a portion of Modern Orthodox Rabbis, Dor Daim and many students of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rambam" title="Rambam">
       Rambam
      </a>
      , completely reject Arizal's Kabbalistic teachings, as well as deny that the
      <i>
       Zohar
      </i>
      is authoritative, or from
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shimon_bar_Yohai" title="Shimon bar Yohai">
       Shimon bar Yohai
      </a>
      , all three of these groups completely accept the existence and validity of
      <i>
       Ma'aseh Merkavah
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       Ma'aseh B'resheet
      </i>
      mysticism. Their only disagreement concerns whether the Kabbalistic teachings promulgated today are accurate representations of those esoteric teachings to which the Talmud refers. Within the Haredi Jewish community one can find both rabbis who sympathise with such a view, while not necessarily agreeing with it,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
       <a href="#cite_note-76">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        76
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      as well as rabbis who consider such a view absolute heresy. Rabbis
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eliyahu_Dessler" title="Eliyahu Dessler">
       Eliyahu Dessler
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Gedaliah_Nadel" title="Gedaliah Nadel">
       Gedaliah Nadel
      </a>
      maintained that it is acceptable to believe that the Zohar was not written by Rabbi Shimon bar Yochai and that it had a late authorship.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
       <a href="#cite_note-77">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        77
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Conservative.2C_Reform_and_Reconstructionist_Judaism">
       Conservative, Reform and Reconstructionist Judaism
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kabbalah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Conservative, Reform and Reconstructionist Judaism">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:137px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lekah_Dodi.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="439" data-file-width="528" height="112" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Lekah_Dodi.jpg/135px-Lekah_Dodi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Lekah_Dodi.jpg/203px-Lekah_Dodi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Lekah_Dodi.jpg/270px-Lekah_Dodi.jpg 2x" width="135"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lekah_Dodi.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A version of
        <a href="/wiki/Lekhah_Dodi" title="Lekhah Dodi">
         Lekhah Dodi
        </a>
        song to welcome the
        <a href="/wiki/Shabbat" title="Shabbat">
         Shabbat
        </a>
        , a cross denomination Jewish custom from Kabbalah
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Kabbalah tended to be rejected by most Jews in the Conservative and
      <a href="/wiki/Reform_Judaism" title="Reform Judaism">
       Reform
      </a>
      movements, though its influences were not completely eliminated. While it was generally not studied as a discipline, the Kabbalistic
      <i>
       Kabbalat Shabbat
      </i>
      service remained part of liberal liturgy, as did the
      <i>
       Yedid Nefesh
      </i>
      prayer. Nevertheless, in the 1960s, Rabbi
      <a href="/wiki/Saul_Lieberman" title="Saul Lieberman">
       Saul Lieberman
      </a>
      of the
      <a href="/wiki/Jewish_Theological_Seminary_of_America" title="Jewish Theological Seminary of America">
       Jewish Theological Seminary of America
      </a>
      is reputed to have introduced a lecture by Scholem on Kabbalah with a statement that Kabbalah itself was "nonsense", but the academic study of Kabbalah was "scholarship". This view became popular among many Jews, who viewed the subject as worthy of study, but who did not accept Kabbalah as teaching literal truths.
     </p>
     <p>
      According to Rabbi
      <a href="/wiki/Bradley_Shavit_Artson" title="Bradley Shavit Artson">
       Bradley Shavit Artson
      </a>
      (Dean of the Conservative
      <a href="/wiki/Ziegler_School_of_Rabbinic_Studies" title="Ziegler School of Rabbinic Studies">
       Ziegler School of Rabbinic Studies
      </a>
      in the
      <a href="/wiki/American_Jewish_University" title="American Jewish University">
       American Jewish University
      </a>
      )
     </p>
     <blockquote class="toccolours" style="float:none; display:table;">
      <div style="padding: 10px 15px 10px 15px;">
       Many western Jews insisted that their future and their freedom required shedding what they perceived as parochial orientalism. They fashioned a Judaism that was decorous and strictly rational (according to 19th-century European standards), denigrating Kabbalah as backward, superstitious, and marginal.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
        <a href="#cite_note-78">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         78
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </div>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      However, in the late 20th century and early 21st century there has been a revival in interest in Kabbalah in all branches of liberal Judaism. The Kabbalistic 12th-century prayer
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Anim_Zemirot" title="Anim Zemirot">
        Anim Zemirot
       </a>
      </i>
      was restored to the new Conservative
      <i>
       Sim Shalom
      </i>
      <i>
       siddur
      </i>
      , as was the
      <i>
       B'rikh Shmeh
      </i>
      passage from the Zohar, and the mystical
      <i>
       Ushpizin
      </i>
      service welcoming to the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Sukkah" title="Sukkah">
        Sukkah
       </a>
      </i>
      the spirits of Jewish forbearers.
      <i>
       Anim Zemirot
      </i>
      and the 16th-century mystical poem
      <i>
       Lekhah Dodi
      </i>
      reappeared in the Reform Siddur
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Gates_of_Prayer" title="Gates of Prayer">
        Gates of Prayer
       </a>
      </i>
      in 1975. All Rabbinical seminaries now teach several courses in Kabbalahin Conservative Judaism, both the Jewish Theological Seminary and the Ziegler School of Rabbinical Studies of the University of Judaism in Los Angeles have full-time instructors in Kabbalah and
      <i>
       Hasidut
      </i>
      , Eitan Fishbane and Pinchas Geller, respectively. In the Reform movement Sharon Koren teaches at the Hebrew Union College. Reform Rabbis like Herbert Weiner and
      <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Kushner" title="Lawrence Kushner">
       Lawrence Kushner
      </a>
      have renewed interest in Kabbalah among Reform Jews. At the Reconstructionist Rabbinical College, the only accredited seminary that has curricular requirements in Kabbalah, Joel Hecker is the full-time instructor teaching courses in Kabbalah and Hasidut.
     </p>
     <p>
      According to Artson:
     </p>
     <blockquote class="toccolours" style="float:none; display:table;">
      <div style="padding: 10px 15px 10px 15px;">
       Ours is an age hungry for meaning, for a sense of belonging, for holiness. In that search, we have returned to the very Kabbalah our predecessors scorned. The stone that the builders rejected has become the head cornerstone (Psalm 118:22)... Kabbalah was the last universal theology adopted by the entire Jewish people, hence faithfulness to our commitment to positive-historical Judaism mandates a reverent receptivity to Kabbalah.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Artson_24-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Artson-24">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         24
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </div>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Reconstructionist_Judaism" title="Reconstructionist Judaism">
       Reconstructionist
      </a>
      movement, under the leadership of Arthur Green in the 1980s and 1990s, and with the influence of Zalman Schachter Shalomi, brought a strong openness to Kabbalah and hasidic elements that then came to play prominent roles in the Kol ha-Neshamah siddur series.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_study">
       Contemporary study
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kabbalah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Contemporary study">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Teaching of classic esoteric kabbalah texts and practice remained traditional until recent times, passed on in Judaism from master to disciple, or studied by leading rabbinic scholars. This changed in the 20th century, through conscious reform and the secular openness of knowledge. In contemporary times kabbalah is studied in four very different, though sometimes overlapping, ways:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       The traditional method, employed among Jews since the 16th century, continues in learned study circles. Its prerequisite is to either be born Jewish or be a convert and to join a group of kabbalists under the tutelage of a rabbi, since the 18th century more likely a Hasidic one, though others exist among Sephardi-Mizrachi, and Lithuanian Rabbinic scholars. Beyond elite, historical esoteric kabbalah, the public-communally studied texts of Hasidic thought explain kabbalistic concepts for wide spiritual application, through their own concern with popular psychological perception of Divine Panentheism. In recent times, many
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orthodox_Jewish_outreach" title="Orthodox Jewish outreach">
        Orthodox Jewish outreach
       </a>
       organisations for secular Jews teach Kabbalistic and Hasidic texts.
      </li>
      <li>
       A second, new universalist form, is the method of modern-style Jewish organisations and writers, who seek to disseminate kabbalah to every man, woman and child regardless of race or class, especially since the Western interest in mysticism from the 1960s. These derive from various cross-denominational Jewish interests in kabbalah, and range from considered theology to popularised forms that often adopt New Age terminology and beliefs for wider communication. These groups highlight or interpret kabbalah through non-particularist, universalist aspects.
      </li>
      <li>
       A third way are non-Jewish organisations, mystery schools, initiation bodies, fraternities and
       <a href="/wiki/Secret_society" title="Secret society">
        secret societies
       </a>
       , the most popular of which are
       <a href="/wiki/Freemasonry" title="Freemasonry">
        Freemasonry
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Rosicrucianism" title="Rosicrucianism">
        Rosicrucianism
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Hermetic_Order_of_the_Golden_Dawn" title="Hermetic Order of the Golden Dawn">
        Golden Dawn
       </a>
       , although hundreds of similar societies claim a kabbalistic lineage. These derive from
       <a href="/wiki/Syncretism" title="Syncretism">
        syncretic
       </a>
       combinations of Jewish kabbalah with Christian, occultist or contemporary New Age spirituality. As a separate spiritual tradition in Western esotericism since the Renaissance, with different aims from its Jewish origin, the non-Jewish traditions differ significantly and do not give an accurate representation of the Jewish spiritual understanding (or vice versa).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
        <a href="#cite_note-79">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         79
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       Fourthly, since the mid-20th century,
       <a href="/wiki/History" title="History">
        historical-critical
       </a>
       scholarly investigation of all eras of Jewish mysticism has flourished into an established department of university
       <a href="/wiki/Jewish_studies" title="Jewish studies">
        Jewish studies
       </a>
       . Where the first academic historians of Judaism in the 19th century opposed and marginalised kabbalah, Gershom Scholem and his successors repositioned the historiography of Jewish mysticism as a central, vital component of Judaic renewal through history. Cross-disciplinary academic revisions of Scholem's and others' theories are regularly published for wide readership.
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Universalist_Jewish_organisations">
       Universalist Jewish organisations
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kabbalah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Universalist Jewish organisations">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The two, unrelated organisations that translate the mid-20th century teachings of Rabbi Yehuda Ashlag into a contemporary universalist message, have given kabbalah a public cross-religious profile:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Bnei_Baruch" title="Bnei Baruch">
         Bnei Baruch
        </a>
       </i>
       is a group of Kabbalah students, based in Israel. Study materials are available in over 25 languages for free online or at printing cost. Michael Laitman established Bnei Baruch in 1991, following the passing of his teacher, Rabbi Ashlag's son Rav
       <a href="/wiki/Baruch_Ashlag" title="Baruch Ashlag">
        Baruch Ashlag
       </a>
       . Laitman named his group Bnei Baruch (sons of Baruch) to commemorate the memory of his mentor. The teaching strongly suggests restricting one's studies to 'authentic sources', kabbalists of the direct lineage of master to disciple.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
        <a href="#cite_note-80">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         80
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
        <a href="#cite_note-81">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         81
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Kabbalah_Centre" title="Kabbalah Centre">
         The Kabbalah Centre
        </a>
       </i>
       was founded in the United States in 1965 as The National Research Institute of Kabbalah by
       <a href="/wiki/Philip_Berg" title="Philip Berg">
        Philip Berg
       </a>
       and Rav Yehuda Tzvi Brandwein, disciple of Rabbi Yehuda Ashlag's. Later Philip Berg and his wife re-established the organisation as the worldwide Kabbalah Centre.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
        <a href="#cite_note-82">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         82
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       In recent times its outreach teaching in New Age style has attracted a cross-religious celebrity following and media profile, though the organisation is led by
       <a href="/wiki/Orthodox_Judaism" title="Orthodox Judaism">
        Orthodox Jewish
       </a>
       teachers.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
        <a href="#cite_note-83">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         83
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      Other prominent Jewish universalist organisations:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Kabbalah_Society" title="Kabbalah Society">
         The Kabbalah Society
        </a>
       </i>
       , run by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Warren_Kenton" title="Warren Kenton">
        Warren Kenton
       </a>
       , an organisation based instead on pre-Lurianic Medieval Kabbalah presented in universalist New Age syncretic style. In contrast, traditional kabbalists read earlier kabbalah through later Lurianism and the systemisations of 16th-century Safed.
      </li>
     </ul>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <i>
        The New Kabbalah
       </i>
       , website and books by Sanford L. Drob, is a scholarly intellectual investigation of the Lurianic symbolism in the perspective of modern and postmodern intellectual thought. It seeks a "new kabbalah" rooted in the historical tradition through its academic study, but universalised through dialogue with modern philosophy and psychology. This approach seeks to enrich the secular disciplines, while uncovering intellectual insights formerly implicit in kabbalah's essential myth:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
        <a href="#cite_note-84">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         84
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <blockquote class="templatequote">
      <p>
       "By being equipped with the nonlinear concepts of dialectical, psychoanalytic, and deconstructive thought we can begin to make sense of the kabbalistic symbols in our own time. So equipped, we are today probably in a better position to understand the philosophical aspects of the kabbalah than were the kabbalists themselves."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
        <a href="#cite_note-85">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         85
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
     </blockquote>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Neo-Hasidic">
       Neo-Hasidic
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kabbalah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Neo-Hasidic">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      From the early 20th century, Neo-Hasidism expressed a non-Orthodox Jewish interest in Jewish mysticism, becoming organisational among Conservative, Reform and Reconstructionalist Jewish denominations from the 1960s, through
      <a href="/wiki/Jewish_Renewal" title="Jewish Renewal">
       Jewish Renewal
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Chavurah" title="Chavurah">
       Chavurah
      </a>
      movement. The writings and teachings of
      <a href="/wiki/Zalman_Schachter-Shalomi" title="Zalman Schachter-Shalomi">
       Zalman Schachter-Shalomi
      </a>
      , Arthur Green, Lawrence Kushner,
      <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Weiner" title="Herbert Weiner">
       Herbert Weiner
      </a>
      and others, has sought a Kabbalistic and Hasidic study and spirituality among modernist Jews. Arthur Green's translations from the religious writings of
      <a href="/wiki/Hillel_Zeitlin" title="Hillel Zeitlin">
       Hillel Zeitlin
      </a>
      conceive the latter to be a precursor of contemporary neo-Hasidism.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Hasidic">
       Hasidic
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kabbalah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Hasidic">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Since the 18th century, Jewish mystical development has continued in Hasidic Judaism, turning kabbalah into a social revival with texts that internalise mystical thought. Among different schools,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chabad-Lubavitch" title="Chabad-Lubavitch">
       Chabad-Lubavitch
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Breslov_(Hasidic_group)" title="Breslov (Hasidic group)">
       Breslav
      </a>
      with related organisations, give outward looking spiritual resources and textual learning for secular Jews. The Intellectual Hasidism of Chabad most emphasises the spread and understanding of kabbalah through its explanation in Hasidic thought, articulating the Divine meaning within kabbalah through human rational analogies, uniting the spiritual and material, esoteric and exoteric in their Divine source:
     </p>
     <blockquote class="templatequote">
      <p>
       "Hasidic thought instructs in the predominance of spiritual form over physical matter, the advantage of matter when it is purified, and the advantage of form when integrated with matter. The two are to be unified so one cannot detect where either begins or ends, for 'the Divine beginning is implanted in the end and the end in the beginning' (Sefer Yetzira 1:7). The One God created both for one purpose to reveal the holy light of His hidden power. Only both united complete the perfection desired by the Creator."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
        <a href="#cite_note-86">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         86
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
     </blockquote>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Rav_Kook">
       Rav Kook
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kabbalah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Rav Kook">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The writings of Abraham Isaac Kook (18641935), first Chief Rabbi of Mandate Palestine and visionary, incorporate kabbalistic themes through his own poetic language and concern with human and divine unity. His influence is in the
      <a href="/wiki/Religious_Zionism" title="Religious Zionism">
       Religious-Zionist
      </a>
      community, who follow his aim that the legal and imaginative aspects of Judaism should interfuse:
     </p>
     <blockquote class="templatequote">
      <p>
       "Due to the alienation from the 'secret of God' [i.e. Kabbalah], the higher qualities of the depths of Godly life are reduced to trivia that do not penetrate the depth of the soul. When this happens, the most mighty force is missing from the soul of nation and individual, and Exile finds favor essentially... We should not negate any conception based on rectitude and awe of Heaven of any formonly the aspect of such an approach that desires to negate the mysteries and their great influence on the spirit of the nation. This is a tragedy that we must combat with counsel and understanding, with holiness and courage."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
        <a href="#cite_note-87">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         87
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
     </blockquote>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">
       See also
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kabbalah&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: See also">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>

      </li>

         <br/>
         (    '  available at
         <a class="external free" href="http://hydepark.hevre.co.il/topic.asp?topic_id=2503514&amp;whichpage=4&amp;forum_id=20067#R_3" rel="nofollow">

       <li id="cite_note-77">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-77">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <a class="external text" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20140830135654/http://www.zootorah.com/controversy/ZoharEnglish.pdf#page=39" rel="nofollow">
          An Analysis of the Authenticity of the Zohar
         </a>
         (2005), p. 39, with "Rav E" and "Rav G" later identified by the author as Rabbi
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eliyahu_Dessler" title="Eliyahu Dessler">
          Eliyahu Dessler
         </a>
         and Rabbi
         <a href="/wiki/Gedaliah_Nadel" title="Gedaliah Nadel">
          Gedaliah Nadel
         </a>
         , respectively (Rabbi Dr.
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marc_Shapiro" title="Marc Shapiro">
          Marc Shapiro
         </a>
         in
         <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20120416154417/http://www.yctorah.org/component/option,com_docman/task,doc_download/gid,1861/" rel="nofollow">
          Milin Havivin Volume 5
         </a>
         [2011], Is there an obligation to believe that Rebbe Shimon bar Yochai wrote the
         <i>
          Zohar
         </i>
         ?, p.  [PDF page 133]):
         <br/>
         "I approached Rav A [Aryeh Carmell] with some of the questions on the Zohar, and he responded to me - 'and what about nikud? Nikud is also mentioned in the Zohar despite the fact that it [is] from Geonic times!' he said. I later found this comment in the Mitpachas Seforim. I would just add that not only is nikud mentioned, but only the Tiberian Nikkud - the norm in Europe of the middle ages - is mentioned and not the Yerushalmi nikud or the Babylonian one  which was used then in the Middle East, and is still used by Yemenites today. Also the Taamay Hamikrah - the trop - are referred to in the Zohar - only by their Sefardi Names. Rav A told me a remarkable piece of testimony: 'My rebbe (this is how he generally refers to Rav E [Elijah Dessler]) accepted the possibility that the Zohar was written sometime in the 13th century.
         <span style="padding-right:.2em;">
          '
         </span>
         "
         <br/>
         "Rav G [Gedaliah Nadel] told me that he was still unsure as to the origin and status of the Zohar, but told me it was my absolute right to draw any conclusions I saw fit regarding both the Zohar and the Ari."
        </span>
       </li>

          </span>
          .
         </span>
         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AKabbalah&amp;rft.atitle=Rabbi+Philip+Berg&amp;rft.date=2013-09-20&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.telegraph.co.uk%2Fnews%2Fobituaries%2F10324260%2FRabbi-Philip-Berg.html&amp;rft.jtitle=Daily+Telegraph&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal">
          <span style="display:none;">
          </span>
         </span>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-84">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-84">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <a class="external text" href="http://www.newkabbalah.com/index3.html" rel="nofollow">
          newkabbalah.com website
         </a>
         including the published English-language books of Sanford Drob
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-85">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-85">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Sanford Drob,
         <i>
          Symbols of the Kabbalah: Philosophical and Psychological Perspectives
         </i>
         , Jason Aronson publishers, p.xvi-xvii. Comparisons of the Lurianic scheme to Hegel, Freud and Jung are treated in respective chapters of Sanford Drob,
         <i>
          Kabbalistic Metaphors: Jewish Mystical Themes in Ancient and Modern Thought
         </i>
         , Aronson. The modern disciplines are explored as particular intellectual/emotional perspectives into the inclusive supra-rational Lurianic symbolism, from which both emerge enriched
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-86">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-86">
           ^
          </a>

        </span>
       </li>
